INDIA
APPRAISAL OF THE
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        Assam with a total area of 78,000 km  and a population of 17 mil-
lion, is the largest State in the north-eastern region. The State can most
easily be described as that portion of the Brahmaputra valley which passes
through India. The valley, about 650 km long from east to west, and 50 to
100 Km wide from north to south, has been formed and remains dominated by the
Brahmaputra, one of the greatest of the world's rivers. The proposed project
is confined mainly to this valley and the smaller Barak valley, which together
contain 97% of Assam's population and grow 95% of its annual crops.
ii.       Production of foodgrains in Assam in the decade to 1974/75 failed to
keep pace with the increase in population. Yields are low, and for the most
part have remained stagnant. Yet potential for increasing foodgrain production
is considerable. Soils are alluvial and fertile; and the high rainfall is
generally well distributed, thus providing scope for substantially increasing
yields and cropping intensity on rainfed areas. Nevertheless irrigation,
though less critical in Assam than in the rest of India, is still important.
While scope for year round surface flow irrigation is limited, most of the
valley has groundwater available within a few meters of the surface, offer-
ing the possibility of an almost limitless source of water which could be
exploited at fairly low cost.
iii.      The project comprises two major components.  The first, the core of
the project, would involve reorganizing and strengthening the agricultural
extension service and developing and upgrading adaptive research, to achieve
early and sustained improvement in agricultural production, particularly
foodgrains. In the past, agricultural development strategy has been aimed
at achieving production increases through use of packages of practices which
assumed the availability of irrigation and required substantial expenditures
on costly purchased inputs such as fertilizer and pesticides. Such recommen-
dations were unsuitable for the majority of Assam's small farmers. The
reorganized extension service would focus first on recommendations to farmers
primarily involving more labor -- an asset most small farmers have in abund-
ance. Aside from generating high economic benefits, the extension and
research components of the project would, therefore, have a considerable
beneficial impact on marginal and small farmers, now living close to the
poverty line. This would be the fourth IDA-supported agricultural extension
and research project in India.



- ii -
iv.       The second component would lay the base for longer term improve-
ments in groundwater development by strengthening the Irrigation Department's
(ID) capacity to plan, administer and supervise programs for exploitation
of Assam's vast groundwater resources. It would include components for
groundwater surveys and monitoring, and technology development to find
alternative low cost approaches to groundwater development. Efforts to
strengthen ID would be focussed through a program of shallow tubewell (STW)
installation. This program would be complementary to but not part of the
proposed project (para v).
v.        Total project costs are estimated at Rs 147.6 M (US$16.4 M).  The
proposed IDA credit of US$8.0 M would be to GOI on standard terms, and would
finance about 50% of project costs, net of duties and taxes. This would cover
foreign exchange costs of US$1.4 M and about 44% of local costs. The balance
of project financing (US$8.4 M) would come from GOA/GOI. The project would,
through strengthening the Irrigation Department, also facilitate implementa-
tion of a complementary groundwater development program. This program would
be carried out under the IDA-supported ARDC line of credit and is expected to
cost US$15.6 M. Finance would come from farmers (US$1.1 M) and commercial and
cooperative banks (US$14.5 M).
vi.       The main items to be financed are:  civil works, vehicles, equip-
ment and incremental staff costs. There would be no international competitive
bidding. Civil works contracts (US$5.1 M) would be small and widely dispersed,
both geographically and over time; and would be awarded on the basis of local
competitive bidding. About 135 motor vehicles of various types (US$1.0 M)
would be required under the project and would be purchased in small quantities
over four years. They would be widely dispersed in rural areas. As adequate
maintenance and availability of spares would be of paramount importance, this
would necessitate purchase of Locally made vehicles of types already widely
used by Government Departments. Procurement would be by local competitive
bidding. Motorcycles and cycles (US$0.3 M) would be purchased by individual
staff according to their preference with loans made available on suitable terms
by GOA. Scientific equipment for research stations (US$0.2 M) and orders for
purchase of minor equipment and furniture (US$0.6 M) would be bulked wherever
possible and purchased following local competitive bidding procedures, except
where orders are valued at less than US$50,000, when they would be purchased by
prudent shopping through normal trade channels. Employment of consultants
(US$0.1 M) would be on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA. The balance
of project costs (US$9.1 M) would consist of staff training (US$1.3 M),
incremental staff salaries and traveling allowances (US$3.5 M), and operating
costs (US$4.3 M).
vii.       Responsibility for implementation of the reorganized extension pro-
gram, the program of adaptive trials, and the groundwater program would be
with the Agricultural Production Commissioner (APC), through the Secretaries
of the Departments of Agriculture and Irrigation. Responsibility for research
at the commodity-oriented research stations would be with the Director of



- iii -
Research, Assam Agricultural University (AAU). The APC would be assisted by
a full time project manager at Deputy Secretary level or above. Development
agencies outside the Departments of Agriculture and Irrigation and AAU, in
particular the lending banks and ARDC, would be coordinated through regular
meetings at State and district level. This would be achieved by establishment
of coordinating committees at state and district levels.
viii.     A Project Coordinating Committee, chaired by the Chief Secretary or
APC, would be set up by November 30, 1977, and would meet at least twice a
year. It would ensure cooperation between various Government organizations,
AAU and the banks and, in particular, that problems of credit, marketing and
input supplies are given due attention.
ix.       Experience in the Chambal and Rajasthan Canal Command Area Develop-
ment Projects indicates that after two years, more than 50% of farm families
followed some or most of the extension recommendations. Based on this assump-
tion, the project could directly benefit about 200,000 contact farmers and
more than 1 M of Assam's 1.9 M farm families within the project period.
Ultimately most of Assam's farmers are expected to benefit. The groundwater
development program, including the technology development program would lay
the base for future development of a larger scale groundwater program, in
particular bringing irrigation within the means of even the smallest
farmers.
x.        The cost of extension and research over the five-year project de-
velopment period amounts to about US$12 per benefitting farm family (assuming
only 1 M farm families benefit). Incremental costs to be met from GOA budget
sources, following project completion, would be about US$1.5 M per annum, or
about US$1.5 per benefitting farm family per annum. This additional require-
ment compares with GOA present budgetary allocation to agriculture of about
US$48 M equivalent annually and would be met without difficulty.
xi.       The principal economic benefit of the project would be an increase
in agricultural production, principally foodgrains. Attributing a precise
level of benefits to this type of project, however, is difficult since the
expected benefits of improved agricultural practices, which also require addi-
tional inputs, cannot be attributed solely to extension and research. More-
over, there is not enough experience in Assam to estimate acceptance rates
with any degree of accuracy. However, there is no doubt that the substantial
improvement in the efficiency of utilization of existing resources, which would
be brought about by the project, would generate a high rate of return. It is
reasonable to expect that by year 7 yields of rice would increase by about
20 kg/ha statewide, or by less than 2% over the "without project" situation.
Given such increases, the economic rate of return would be 50%. Experience
with reorganized extension services in other States of India suggests that
there would be little risk that yields would not increase by an average of at
least 20 kg/ha as a result of the project. The high rate of return should,
however, be treated with caution since, as improved agricultural practices
spread, further investments in infrastructure and services would become
necessary.
xii.      The project is considered suitable for an IDA credit of US$8.0 M
to GOI.






INDIA
APPRAISAL OF THE
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Eastern Region of India, comprising the States of Assam, Bihar,
Orissa, West Bengal and eastern Uttar Pradesh, is the country's major rice
growing area. Most of the region has moderate to heavy monsoon rains and
large areas of relatively fertile alluvial soils, and there is a vast, mainly
unexploited, surface and ground water resource. Yet foodgrain yields are gen-
erally lower than elsewhere in India and production has barely kept pace with
population growth. Rural institutions are generally weak, and most indicators
of social and economic development are lower than for the rest of India. Dur-
ing discussions between GOI and the Bank in New Delhi in November, 1974, it
was agreed to give special emphasis to the development of agricultural pro-
jects in the Eastern Region States aimed to achieve quick increases in food-
grain production while paying particular attention to problems faced by small
farmers. This led to a State by State review of agriculture in the Eastern
States including one in Assam in March 1976. The proposed project is the
third identified in line with this new direction of Bank Group agriculture
operations in India, following the Orissa Agricultural Development Project
(Cr 682-IN) and the West Bengal Agricultural Extension and Research Project
(Cr 690-IN) and is the fourth IDA-supported agricultural extension and
research project in India.  It was prepared by Government of Assam (GOA)
assisted by the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program, Bank staff and consultants.
1.02      This appraisal report is based on the findings of a Mission which
visited Assam in November 1976, comprising Messrs. N. Krafft, S. Allison,
J. Borthwick and J. Harrison (IDA), D. Mohanakrishnan (ARDC), and Mr. F.
Haworth (Consultant). The Mission was assisted full-time by Mr. S. Hocombe
(FAO/CP), who also contributed to the report, and part-time by Messrs. S.
Baker, and U. Hpu and Ms. M. Nguyen (IDA).
II. BACKGROUND
A.  Agriculture in Assam
2.01      Assam, with a total area of 78,000 km  and a population of 17 mil-
lion, is the largest State of the north eastern region, an isolated group of
five States and two union territories bounded by Bhutan and Tibet/China to the
North, Burma to the east and Bangladesh to the South. Assam can most easily
be described as that portion of the Brahmaputra valley which passes through
India. The valley, about 650 Km long from east to west, and 50 to 100 Km wide
from north to south, has been formed and remains dominated by the Brahmaputra,
one of the greatest of the world's rivers. Each year, with the melting of
the snows from the Himalayas and the torrential monsoon rains (usually over



2000 mm) the river swells to ten times its normal winter flow.  Repeated year
after year, this process has built up the valley floor with layers of sand,  2
silt and gravel until there are now rich alluvial deposits covering 50,000 Km
Separated from the Brahmaputra valley by the Mikir and North Cachar hills is
the smaller Southern Barak valley (Cachar district). This project is confined
mainly to these two broad alluvial valleys, which comprise the 8 administrative
plains districts of Assam, contain 97% of its population and grow 95% of its
annual crops.
Land Tenure and Distribution
2.02      Many of the problems of raising agricultural productivity in Assam
stem from the large number of small and fragmented farms. Apart from the tea
estates, there are virtually no large holdings in Assam. Average farm size
is 1.5 ha with over 90% of holdings less than 3 ha and nearly 60% less than
1 ha. Sharecropping and tenancy arrangements are still widespread despite
land tenure legislation. Many of the owners of these small farms are poor
and unable to afford expensive inputs or major investments, such as tubewells
on an individual basis. The proposed project would help tackle problems faced
by these small farmers by providing them, through the extension service, with
suitable low cost technology to increase yields and cash income (Para 2.15).
Agriculture (Annex 1)
2.03      The economy of Assam is heavily dependent on agriculture.  In 1971,
the agricultural sector contribu,ted over 57% of total State income and pro-
vided employment for nearly 77% of the total working force. In the plains
districts, 2.2 M ha are cultivated with a cropping intensity of about 120%
giving a total cropped area of 2.7 M ha. Rice is the predominant foodgrain
and accounts for about 80% of total cropped area and over 90% of the area
under foodgrains. The most important commercial crops are tea, oilseeds,
jute, and sugarcane, with Assam producing a little over half of India's total
production of tea mainly from hill districts outside the project area. Pro-
duction of foodgrains in the decade to 1974/75 increased at about 1.7% p.a.
failing to keep pace with the increase in population. The little increase in
foodgrain production that has taken place, resulted mainly from increases in
cropped area. Agricultural yields have remained stagnant. Cultural practices
are still largely traditional and few farmers have any experience in using
modern inputs. Average fertilizer use (excluding that for tea) is less than
3 Kg of nutrient per ha; only about 10% of rice is under HYV or improved local
varieties; and most farmers still rely on traditional farm implements.
2.04      Potential.  In contrast to past performance, potential for increasing
foodgrain production is considerable. Soils are alluvial and fertile; and
the high rainfall is generally well distributed, occurring during a 7-month
monsoon season, providing scope for substantially increasing yields on rain-
fed areas. Because of humid and relatively cool conditions in the remaining
5 months, it is generally possible to grow a second rainfed crop on residual
moisture, albeit with low yields. However, in some areas the dry months are
often too dry to sustain reliable crop growth without supplemental water.
Even during the monsoon, but particularly at the beginning and end, there



-3-
are dry spells of sufficient length to limit crop growth. Thus, irrigation,
though less critical in Assam than in the rest of India, is also important to
realizing full agricultural potential. While scope for year-round surface
flow irrigation is limited, most of the valley has groundwater available with-
in a few meters of the surface (para 2.09 and Annex 4). This great reservoir
offers the possibility of an almost limitless source of water which could be
exploited at fairly low cost. The strategy for future development is dis-
cussed in paras 2.15 - 2.17.
Constraints to Development
2.05      The reasons for the gap between performance and potential are
two-fold: technical and institutional. The main technical problems arise
from Assam's high rainfall. Up to 15% of the cultivated area may be inundated
by the Brahmaputra River in the monsoon season and, even in flood free areas,
the high rainfall causes problems of drainage, discouraging use of fertilizers
and restricting access to fields. It also creates difficulties for drying and
storing early-planted crops which mature at the height of the monsoon. The
variability in rainfall at both ends of the monsoon are a further disincentive
to use of purchased inputs. These natural constraints, while important, are
not sufficient to explain Assam's low agricultural productivity. As discussed
below, the most severe constraints to development are to be found in the
weaknesses of institutions serving agriculture.
B. Supporting Institutions
Agricultural Extension
2.06      The Director of Agriculture, working under the Agricultural Produc-
tion Commissioner, is responsible for agricultural extension and is assisted
by an Additional Director of Agriculture (General), a number of Joint and
Deputy Directors, and Agricultural Officers at the district and subdivisional
level. Responsibility for field level extension activities is, however, di-
vided between the Department of Community Development and Panchayats, through
the Block Development Officer (BDO) 1/, and the Department of Agriculture,
through the Agricultural Extension Officer (AEO). The AEO is meant to guide
and supervise the work of about 10 multi-purpose Village Level Workers (VLW)
per block but has no administrative control over them. As a result the VLW,
who is directly responsible to the BDO in Community Development, spends only
a fraction of his time on agriculture and much of this on non-productive re-
port writing and regulatory work. The Department of Agriculture, finding it
difficult, therefore, to implement agricultural programs using these multi-
purpose workers came increasingly to rely on departmental staff appointed for
1/   Assam has 8 plains' administrative districts (9 agricultural) each
headed by a Deputy Commissioner; 20 administrative subdivisions
(21 agricultural); and 120 Development Blocks, the local rural
administrative unit headed by a BDO. BDO is directly responsible
to the Deputy Commissioner for development programs of the different
State departments (animal husbandry, public health, education, etc.).



-4-
"special schemes". Special schemes, however, have only served to weaken the
effects of extension staff still further, dissipating efforts and obscuring
the need for fundamental reform. This has all resulted in few farm level
contacts and low impact on yields, particularly of small farmers.
2.07      Training of Agricultural Staff:  AEO are generally direct recruits
from colleges who have received minimal in-service training. VLW are gen-
erally secondary school graduates, recruited after a two years' pre-service
course given at one of the Department of Agriculture's two Gramsevak Training
Centers. There is limited in-service training for VLW and many older AEO and
VLW received their basic training before many of the recent technological
advances.
Agricultural Research
2.08      Until 1973, the main responsibility for agricultural research rested
with the Department of Agriculture. Responsibility for both fundamental and
applied research has since been transferred to Assam Agricultural University
(AAU), a young institution with a weak but developing research program, estab-
lished under the AAU Act of 1968. The IDA-financed First Education Project
(Credit 342-IN) is assisting AAU's institutional development through building
up AAU's teaching capability and physical facilities at its two main campuses
(Jorhat and Khanapara). Now that these campuses are becoming fully opera-
tional and more staff are returning from training fellowships provided under
the Credit, AAU is in a position to focus on strengthening its research
capability both at its central and regional research stations. Research has
largely concentrated on producing recommendations for irrigated rabi crops,
using HYV and high levels of fertilizer application. Insufficient attention
has been paid to kharif rainfed crops and to providing small farmers with
recommendations as to how they might significantly increase yields with little
or no purchased inputs. Centrally sponsored Indian Council of Agricultural
Research (ICAR) schemes, whilst responsible for the major research impact, are
more oriented to national requirements than to State and regional needs. AAU
staff have normally devoted most of their time to teaching, resulting in
insufficient attention on research. Moreover, many sanctioned research posts
remain vacant due to shortage of adequately trained staff (Annex 3, Table 1).
These shortages, combined with poor physical facilities on the commodity-
oriented research stations and sub-stations have seriously hindered development
of an effective research effort. Finally, there is a lack of research staff
and facilities to test, evaluate and adapt improved agronomic practices in the
different agroclimatic regions of the State.
Irrigation
2.09      Groundwater.  Exploitation of Assam's groundwater resources has
been insignificant -- with only about 30 deep wells and 1,400 shallow tube-
wells (STW) in the State, irrigating less than 5,000 ha. There are several
reasons for this. Until recently, the main focus of the Irrigation Department,
responsible for both groundwater and surface irrigation development, has been
on flood protection. The Department's capacity to plan, supervise and monitor
groundwater development is limited and there has been an almost total lack of



- 5 -
coordination between implementing agencies (Irrigation and Agriculture Depart-
ments, equipment suppliers, well drillers, and, in particular, credit institu-
tions). In addition, since the orthodox STW irrigates 3-5 ha, it is most
suited to larger farms (about 40% of the land), thus bypassing the majority
of farmers in Assam. And water sharing, which would overcome this problem, is
generally difficult to achieve in practice. Future development strategy is
discussed in para 2.16.
2.10      Surface Irrigation.  The Irrigation Department's past emphasis on
flood protection combined with limited opportunities for water storage and
diversion, has also restricted development of surface irrigation. Less than
150,000 ha are presently irrigated and even in these areas yields are low.
Existing surface irrigation schemes have not achieved anticipated benefits
due mainly to inadequate development of infrastructure (field channels and
drains) below major outlets. GOA has limited experience in Command Area
Development (CAD), and the necessary legislation to enable GOA to implement
any large-scale CAD program and to recover costs has yet to be passed. GOA
are currently undertaking two pilot projects to gain practical experience of
CAD. Future investment in surface irrigation is discussed in para 2.17.
Agricultural Credit, Inputs and Marketing
2.11      Credit.  Institutional credit for agriculture is provided by coop-
erative credit institutions and commercial banks. The cooperative credit
structure has two wings: the Assam Cooperative Apex Bank providing short
and medium term credit, and the Assam State Cooperative Land Mortgage Bank,
long term credit. Until recently, short and medium term credit was provided
to farmers through about 3,000 primary agricultural credit societies. By
1972, however, overdues of the Apex Bank reached 71% and those of the primary
credit societies, 81%. Moreover, three-quarters of these societies were dor-
mant. As a result, in 1972, the Reserve Bank of India (RBI) prepared a master
plan to restructure cooperative credit in the State. Following the RBI plan,
GOA replaced the largely defunct primary credit societies with about 660 multi-
purpose cooperatives; but most have yet to make a significant impact in terms
of either credit or input supplies. Fortunately, GOA is now acutely aware of
problems in the cooperative credit system and it has taken several significant
steps to overcome them. However, several RBI recommendations have still to be
implemented and lending to farmers remains small, while past overdues remain
high. Rehabilitation of the cooperative credit system which will require firm
Government support for collection of overdues, upgrading managerial capabili-
ties, streamlining operational procedures and rationalization of the credit
institutions will be lengthy and it is important that efforts start now (para
5.09). Since the project would rely mainly on improved cultural practices at
the start, which would generate a gradual build up in demand for credit, short-
term credit is unlikely to be a constraint in early years.
2.12      Some 19 commercial banks, providing both short and long term cre-
dit, operate in Assam -- with 270 branches, about 230 rural, the remainder
urban. Again, lending has been low and outstanding loans for agricultural
purposes amounted to Rs 24 M in June 1976. Recovery of overdues has also
been small. Recovery performance has improved, however, with the recent



-6-
implementation of that part of the Talwar Committee 1/ recommendations dealing
with recovery of loans. In view of the weakness of the cooperative credit
structure, commercial banks will have to be relied upon to meet most credit
required for groundwater development. These institutions would be able to meet
this demand and this is evidenced by the marked success ARDC has had in for-
mulating and sanctioning schemes for STW, in response to recent emphasis given
to groundwater development (para 2.16). Since March 1976, ARDC has formulated
six schemes for 5,500 STW; four have been sanctioned and refinance of Rs 340,000
disbursed by ARDC to commercial banks.
2.13      Fertilizer, Seeds and Pesticides.  Average fertilizer use at about
3 Kg/ha of nutrients is well below the all-India average of about 16 Kg/ha.
Distribution is through the Assam Agro-Industries Development Corporation
(AAIDC), the Fertilizer Corporation of India (FCI), the recently established
network of multi-purpose cooperatives, and a network of private dealers.
Storage capacity is adequate and any expansion would be constructed through
finance provided under State or Centrally-sponsored schemes. Sales of cer-
tified seed are also low, less than 4,000 tons in 1974/75, but future avail-
ability presents no foreseeable problems with the recent rationalization and
proposed expansion of the seed industry under the Bank-assisted National Seed
Project (1273-IN). A major constraint to wider use of pesticides relates to
the weakness of the extension services. Provision of plant protection spe-
cialists under the project (para 3.05) should help remedy this. While there
should be no major problems meeting the low level of input requirements in the
short run, problems could arise in the longer run requiring a strengthening of
the distribution system. Experience in other States, and in Nowgong subdivi-
sion in Assam where the new extension approach was introduced over a year ago,
indicates that supply institutions respond quickly to demand generated through
the extension service, and that the increased demand when translated into
higher turnover, itself helps supply agencies become more commercially viable.
2.14      Marketing and Procurement.  Agricultural production and marketing
in the past has been dominated by rice and jute; by 1975/76, however, wheat
had gained importance. The Assam State Cooperative Consumer and Marketing
Federation and the Food Corporation of India are charged with procurement of
grain. In 1972, GOA passed the Assam Agricultural Produce Marketing Act
enabling setting up of regulated markets and ARDC is associated with devel-
opment of four such markets. The outcome of this pilot operation would
provide guidelines for future market development. While there are no overall
marketing or storage problems, the situation will have to be carefully moni-
tored as production increases so that additional capacity is provided in a
timely manner.
1/   In September 1969, the Governor of the RBI appointed an Expert Group
to review legislation impinging upon commercial bank lending for agri-
culture. The final report, issued in 1971, is known as the Talwar
report.



-7-
C. Strategy for Development
2.15      In proposing a strategy for increasing foodgrains production, prior-
ity is given to measures which can have a quick impact on the production and
incomes of small farmers who comprise most of the rural population. Since the
overwhelming proportion of farmers in the plains districts are wholly dependent
on rainfall, the emphasis in the short-term must be on improving yields under
rainfed conditions. Moreover, since most small farmers have little or no
experience of modern inputs, early efforts to increase yields will have to
be placed on raising yields largely through better cultural practices such as
seed treatment, timely sowing, optimal plant populations and weeding. The
main priority for increasing agricultural production thus lies in closing the
gap between the present practices of the majority of farmers and existing
technology, demonstrated by the best farmers, by transferring to them the
knowhow of already proven but simple improvements and making better use of
existing inputs and water. This requires a quick and efficient mechanism
for transferring technical knowledge and can largely be achieved by improve-
ments to present agricultural extension methods. However, such improvements
need to be backed by an effective and responsive adaptive research program as
a successful extension service will generate demand for more sophisticated
advice. The gap between existing technology and typical farm practices is
currently so wide that present knowledge will suffice for the next two to
three years, particularly if backed by a program of field trials to adapt
present knowledge to each locality. Additional research is, however, needed
to bring new technologies forward after that time.
2.16      In the longer term there is a need to expand the area under irriga-
tion. The Bank's agricultural sector mission placed emphasis on implemen-
tation of a small first phase program of STW installation. Implementation of
this program has started. To ensure that this is effectively carried out, and
thus lays the framework for possible much larger expansion of the area under
irrigation in the future, the present strategy provides for strengthening of
institutions involved in groundwater development, while carefully monitoring
benefits of such investment. At the same time, it is essential to develop a
technology suitable for small farmers by providing either smaller or cheaper
tubewells and pumps.
2.17      The proposed project would adopt the above strategy.  It does not
attempt to tackle the longer term problems of the input supply institutions,
which are not seen as a constraint to implementation of the proposed project
over 5 years. Regarding surface irrigation, before undertaking any major
investment it would first be necessary to ensure better utilization of water
from existing irrigation schemes. This would be based on experience to be
gained from the two pilot CAD schemes currently being implemented. It will
also be necessary to complete a detailed survey of the surface irrigation and
drainage potential which should lead to a pipeline of projects, each with an
acceptable economic rate of return and high development priority. This survey
could be undertaken by the Irrigation Department, in consultation with the
Central Water Commission. During negotiations, GOA stated that it hoped to
be able to prepare such projects for inclusion into the Sixth Five-Year Plan.



- 8 -
III. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
3.01      The project comprises two major components.  The first, the core
of the project, would involve reorganizing and strengthening the agricultural
extension service and developing and upgrading adaptive research in the plains
districts of Assam, to achieve early and sustained improvement in agricultural
production, particularly foodgrains. The second, would lay the base for longer
term improvements in groundwater development by strengthening the Irrigation
Department and would include a component for technology development to find
alternative low-cost approaches to groundwater development, suitable for small
farmers. This would be the fourth IDA-supported agricultural extension and
research project in India.
3.02      The five-year project would comprise:
(a) Reorganization and strengthening of agricultural extension
services: provision of additional staff, housing, offices,
equipment, transportation, and staff training to complete
by June 30, 1980, the reorganization of the extension ser-
vice started in 1976.
(b) Development and strengthening of adaptive research: establish-
ment of about nine field trials stations by the Department of
Agriculture, and development and upgrading of two commodity-
oriented research stations and one sub-station of AAU, by:
farm development, provision of staff and staff training,
laboratory facilities, housing, hostel accomodation, equip-
ment, vehicles, and about 12 man-months of consultancy services.
(c) Groundwater development: provision of staff and staff training,
vehicles and equipment to strengthen the Irrigation Department
and enable it to: (i) oversee a program of STW installation, which
would be financed by long-term credit operations; (ii) carry out a
program of groundwater surveys and monitoring, and (iii) install,
test and demonstrate alternative, low-cost approaches to ground-
water development.
(d) Project monitoring and evaluation: Provision of staff, equip-
ment and funds for studies to monitor project progress and
evaluate its impact.
B. Detailed Features
Agricultural Extension (Annex 2)
3.03      The Extension Methodology:  The project would complete the reor-
ganization and intensification of agricultural extension started in one



- 9 -
district of Assam in 1976. This would be confined to the plains districts
of Assam. The introduction of the proposed extension system would be phased
over three years district by district and would be completed by June 30, 1980.
The objective would be to provide farmers on a regular and systematic basis
with up-to-date advice on farming practices capable of having an immediate
impact on yields. The primary instrument to bring about this change would be
full-time village level agricultural extension workers (Para 3.04). Each of
these extension workers (VLEW) would cover some 600 to 1000 farm families,
divided into 8 smaller groups of about 100 families, the number varying
according to population density and communication network. Each group would
be visited by its VLEW on a fixed day of the week, once every two weeks. Each
VLEW wou'ld work primarily with about ten contact farmers selected from each
group. Other farmers in the group would be encouraged to join the VLEW in his
field visits and discussion sessions. The message communicated to farmers
would be simple, concentrating on the most important crops of their locality
and initially on improved cultural practices of relevance to current and up-
coming farm operations. The VLEW and AEO would receive the extension message
at training sessions held one day in the two-week period by Subject Matter
Specialists. These messages would be prepared by district technical commit-
tees (para 5.05). During the following week, VLEW would meet with their
immediate supervisor, the AEO, to discuss problems encountered in the field.
On the two days remaining in the two-week period (one rest day a week), the
VLEW would participate in demonstrations, field trials and administrative
work.
3.04      The crux of the reorganized extension program would be a direct line
of command from the Director of Agriculture through to VLEW. This would be
achieved by transferring 80% of the multipurpose village level workers (VLW)
from the Department of Community Development and Panchayats to the Department
of Agriculture (DOA), incorporating staff from "special schemes"? and redeploy-
ing some administrative and regulatory staff. This would provide 1660 VLEW.
Under the project, an additional 780 VLEW would be recruited over a three-year
period, providing 2440 VLEW to cover Assam's nearly 2 million farm families
(average ratio of 1:800). Some 305 AEO, already part of the Department of
Agriculture, would supervise and support the VLEW (ratio 1:8) and they, in
turn, would be responsible to the Subdivisional Agricultural Officer (Para
3.05). GOA has issued an order setting out details of its program for (i)
transfer of 80% of VLW (about 960 VLW) from Community Development to the
Department of Agriculture and (ii) reassignment of present staff from special
schemes.   When the transfer is completed, each block would be left with a
small field staff, usually one AEO and two VLW to cover non-agricultural tasks
and to ensure the timely flow of credit and inputs.
3.05      Technical Support:  A number of Assam's subdivisions are too large
to allow for effective supervision, training and control of field staff. Six
of the existing 21 agricultural subdivisions would, therefore, be divided,
thus creating 27 subdivisions. Each would be headed by a Subdivisional
Agricultural Officer (SDAO). In cases where one SDAO would supervise more
than 8 AEO, an Additional SDAO (ASDAO) would also be appointed. To strengthen
technical backup, the project provides for two subject matter specialists
(SMS) at each subdivision, one each in agronomy and plant protection. In



- 10 -
subdivisions in which the shallow tubewell program is being implemented
(para 3.12) an additional SMS (water management) would be provided. At dis-
trict level, the project provides for three senior SMS, one in information
and training and two to give technical support in such matters as agronomy,
water management, farm implements, entomology and pathology. Specialists
would spend their time about equally between field visits including checks
on lower level staff; visiting nearby adaptive field trial stations (para 3.08
ff); and training. Provision under the project has also been made for other
supervisory and supporting staff (Annex 7, Table 5) including a team of four
SMS to be located at headquarters (para 5.02) and strengthening of adminis-
trative and accounting staff in DOA. Staff would be appointed in accordance
with the schedule in Annex 12, Table 2. Based on experience in other states,
extension staff would work harder than at present; provision has, therefore,
been made for advanced increments in salary to be awarded to about 25% of VLEW
and other selected staff.
3.06      Training:  Fortnightly training of VLEW by SMS with assistance of
AEO would be a major feature of the extension system. AEO would be trained
for one day each month by subdivisional staff, backed up by staff from the
district and field trial stations (para 3.08 ff). Limited equipment and
extension aids would be required for staff training and these would be included
in the project. At the start of the project new staff would have to be trained
and others upgraded. The project provides for pre-service training fellowships
for SMS, inservice and short-term training, and scholarships for advancement
training for a few staff. It also provides for incremental staff at the two
existing training centers to enable them cope with the increased enrollment.
3.07      Housing, Offices and Transportation:  The proposed extension metho-
logy is based on regular and frequent farm visits by extension staff. It is
essential, therefore, that field level staff and their supervisors live near
their work and that they are sufficiently mobile to enable them to function
effectively. At present, housing available for rent in rural areas is lim-
ited and extension workers rely on public transport, thus spending much of
their time traveling. Assurances were given that GOA would ensure that
field level staff would live in the area assigned for their operations and,
for this purpose, GOA would provide such low-cost housing as necessary. The
project provides for renovation of existing housing that is suitably located,
and construction of low cost housing so that, at full development, housing
would be provided for about 60% of VLEW and 50% of AEO and subdivisional
staff. Designs for such housing have been reviewed by IDA and are satisfac-
tory. The remainder have satisfactory housing available. The project would
finance construction of subdivisional offices in the six subdivisions created
under the project, their equipment and furniture. Selection of sites for
housing and offices has not, as yet, been completed; assurances were given
that by March 31, 1978, or such later date as may be agreed by the Associa-
tion, GOA would complete a survey to locate such sites. The project also pro-
vides for additional equipment and vehicles at headquarters, subdivisional,
and district levels, including motorcycles for AEO and bicycles for VLEW.
Credit would be provided to staff by GOA to enable them to purchase motor-
cycles and cycles. GOA is presently reviewing traveling allowances, credit



- 11 -
limits, and terms and conditions of loans. Assurances were given that GOA
would (i) provide credit to staff for purchase of motorcycles and bicycles on
such terms and conditions that would create incentive for the purchase of such
transport, and (ii) provide such financial incentive as is required to ensure
optimum use of such transport. Provision has been made in project costs for
operating costs of incremental departmental vehicles and incremental staff
traveling allowances.
Adaptive Research and Trials (Annex 3)
3.08      Under the project, adaptive research facilities would be set up or
strengthened to provide the extension service with simple and relevant tech-
nological messages suitably adapted to locality, size of farm, and ability
of the farmer to invest and take risks. To achieve this, a network of nine
field trials (and training) stations (FTS) would be established under the
Department of Agriculture. To ensure the flow of technical innovations to
the farmer in the longer run, these would need to be supported by an expanded
capability for production-oriented crop research. The project would also
strengthen two of AAU's commodity-oriented research stations and one sub-
station -- located at Titabar and Karimganj (for work on rice) and at
Shillangani (for work on jute, oilseeds, pulses and wheat). The project also
provides for establishment of a small cell of DOA specialists stationed at
AAU to oversee work on FTS (Para 5.03).
3.09      The commodity-oriented research program of AAU would seek to
identify and select improved varieties of the most important crops obtained
from national and international research institutions, with a package of eco-
nomically significant practices directed to substantially increasing crop
production and at the same time minimize farmers risk. Selected varieties
and production packages would be forwarded to FTS for further testing in each
of the different agroecological zones, concentrating initially on rice, jute
and wheat. At FTS, the most promising practices and varieties would be iden-
tified and recommendations prepared for the extension service. FTS staff
would work closely with a district extension team, in particular SMS, and much
of the work at FTS would be done in response to needs of the local extension
staff and farmers. FTS would thus be the focal point for testing and intro-
duction of new practices and would also be used for training of extension
staff.
3.10      The project would provide for civil works, including the construction
of laboratory, farm and office buildings, staff housing and station develop-
ment as required (drainage, water and electricity supplies, fencing, roads).
Four sites chosen for establishment of FTS have modest dormitory and training
facilities; these would be improved where necessary and new units constructed
on the remaining FTS stations to facilitate staff and farmer training. The
project would also finance laboratory, farm and office equipment, limited
office and laboratory furniture and about 17 vehicles. Incremental salaries,
allowances and other operating costs amounting to 34% of total research costs
(58% for FTS and 17% for MAU stations) would also be financed as part of the
project.



- 12 -
3.11      Staffing, Training and Consultancy Services:  At full development,
professional staff at the commodity-oriented stations of AMU would be in-
creased from 58 to 73. Some posts already sanctioned for these stations are
vacant. If a post to be provided under the project is in the same discipline
as an already sanctioned but vacant post, the vacant post would be filled
first in accordance with the schedule in Annex 12, Table 2. Each FTS would be
staffed with three research officers under an agronomist and comprising an
entomologist, a plant pathologist, and either a farm management specialist or
soil scientist, as appropriate. The project would, therefore, provide 40 new
professional staff for the nine FTS and the four person cell. Support staff
would also be provided. Staff would be appointed in accordance with the
schedule in Annex 12, Table 2. To provide intensive training in relevant
research the project would provide for: fellowships for staff of the FTS and
adaptive trials cell of 2-3 months, 18 fellowships to train staff for sanc-
tioned but currently vacant posts at AAU, and 10 fellowships for new AAU posts
created under the project. The project would also provide for about 12
man-months of local consultancy services (para 5.04).
Groundwater Development (Annex 4)
3.12      Complementary Development.  Efforts to strengthen the Irrigation
Department's (ID) capacity to plan, administer and supervise programs for
exploitation of Assam's vast groundwater resource would be focussed through
a program of STW installation. This program, which has already started, would
comprise installation of about: 15,000 pumpsets, each irrigating 3-5 ha, and
would be complementary to but not part of the proposed project (para 4.03).
About 12,500 pumpsets would be mounted on low cost shallow tubewells; the
remainder would pump directly from surface water sources (streams and ponds).
To concentrate administrative resources, the program would be confined to
three areas, comprising 20 development blocks in the central and western
parts of the Brahmaputra valley (Map 12626), and would be phased over four
years.
3.13      To ensure that this program is carried out effectively, the project
provides for strengthening of ID, to enable it to supervise: supply, installa-
tion and maintenance of pumpsets. The project would provide for limited low
level staff, training for incremental and existing staff, vehicles for super-
visory staff, about 50 bicycles for mechanics, and survey and drawing equipment.
It would also provide four tractors with trailors to transport well drilling
equipment, downwell components, and pumpsets between sites. Staff would also
be appointed to help farmers process and submit applications and necessary
supporting documentation and to advise farmers on engineering aspects of
efficient on-farm water utilization, such as land levelling and field channel
construction. Staff would be appointed in accordance with the Schedule in
Annex 12, Table 2.
3.14      Groundwater Surveys and Monitoring.  Because the proposed comple-
mentary groundwater program is small, risk of interference between wells would
be negligible. As groundwater development increases so would groundwater
extraction. To ensure orderly development and to avoid over-exploitation in
the long run, the project would include a three-year program of groundwater



- 13 -
surveys and monitoring to strengthen both GOA's information base for planning
future projects and its capacity to monitor the hydrologic effects of ground-
water development. To achieve this, the project would include: (a) instal-
lation of 220 small bore observation wells on a 16 km square grid covering
virtually the entire Brahmaputra Valley, to permit clearer definition of
aquifer materials and observation of fluctuations in the water table; (b)
execution of a series of pumping tests on existing deep wells and new shallow
wells, to provide more accurate estimates of yield and transmissibility, and
(c) a mapping program, in the 20 selected blocks to permit well locations to
be accurately recorded and drawdown patterns monitored. The project would
provide for installation of observation wells, equipment and vehicles, staff
to collect and test aquifer samples and conduct pumping test, and drafting
and survey equipment for the mapping program.
3.15      Technology Development.  The STW to be installed are the smallest
possible with existing equipment. However, because they irrigate 3-5 ha,
they are generally too large (and costly) for the smallest farms. The project
would, therefore, include a program of research, design, installation and
field testing to provide alternative technologies suitable for the smaller
farms. It would include development of (a) small diameter low cost well
points, made of either bamboo or thin-walled corrugated PVC; (b) small capa-
city pumps, either manually or (where rural electrification is available)
electrically operated; and (c) custom services schemes using small mobile
pumpsets serving a group of wells on a rental basis. The project would
provide for manufacture by the Irrigation Department of new type bamboo
screens and manual pumps (pendulum and rotary), electrification, installation
of wells and wellpoints, tubewells and pump components, equipment and staff.
Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 5)
3.16      In addition to improvement of Assam's groundwater monitoring
capability (Para 3.14), an essential feature of the project would be the
monitoring and evaluation of project progress and its impact. Monitoring
consists of the timely gathering and analysis of data to measure the build
up and performance of the various organizations supported under the project.
Monitoring of physical and financial inputs (recruitment of staff, provision
of transportation, equipment, housing, installation of STW etc.) would largely
be carried out through the normal reporting systems of the concerned Depart-
ments. However, this information needs to be supplemented to provide an
independent assessment of performance of project organizations and of farmers'
reaction to recommended practices. For this purpose, and to evaluate project
impact (increased production resulting from the reorganized extension service
and from investment in STW, increased farmers' income and distribution, mea-
surement of STW costs and benefits etc.) the project would provide for staff,
equipment and funds for surveys (para 5.10).



- 14 -

4.02      Cost estimates are based on findings during appraisal and relate
to prices in April 1977. Physical contingencies have been applied at appro-
priate rates (up to 15%) on civil works, equipment and furniture and average
about 7% on total basic project cost. Based on Bank estimates of future price
movements in India, price contingencies have been applied cumula.lvely as
follows (Annex 11):
(a) Local equipment, vehicles, training, and incremental salaries
and operating costs: 6% in 1977, and 7% per annum thereafter;
(b) Civil works: 7% in 1977 and 8% per annum thereafter; and
(c) Imported equipment: 7.5% per annum 1977-79, and 7%
thereafter.
B. Proposed Financing
4.03      Finance to cover total project costs of Rs 146.7 M (US$16.4 M),
would be provided as follows:
IDA     GOA/GOI   Total
US$ Million ----
8.0       8.4       16.4
(49%)     (51%)    (100%)
The proposed IDA credit of US$8.0 M would be to GOI on standard terms, and
would finance about 50% of project costs, net of duties and taxes.  This would
cover foreign exchange costs of US$1.4 M and about 44% of local costs. IDA
funds would be channeled through GOI to GOA in accordance with GOI standard
terms and arrangements for financing of State development projects. The bal-
ance of project financing (US$8.4 M) would come from GOA/GOI. The project
would, through strengthening the Irrigation Department, also facilitate imple-
mentation of a complementary groundwater development program. This program
would be carried out under the IDA-supported ARDC line of credit and is ex-
pected to cost US$15.6 M. Finance would come from farmers (US$1.1 M), and
commercial and cooperative banks (US$14.5 M).
C. Procurement
4.04      The main items to be procured are:  civil works; vehicles, includ-
ing motorcycles and bicycles; and equipment. There would be no international
competitive bidding. Civil works range from small low-cost houses for VLEW to
larger research buildings.  Civil works contracts (US$5.1 M) would be small and
dispersed, both geographically and over time, and would not be suitable for
international competitive bidding. Contracts would be awarded on the basis of
competitive bidding following local advertising and assurances were obtained
accordingly. There are numerous qualified local contractors to carry out civil
works.



- 16 -
4.05      About 135 motor vehicles of various types (US$1.0 M) would be
required under the project. They would be purchased in small quantities
over four years and would be widely dispersed in rural areas. As adequate
maintenance and availability of spares would be of paramount importance,
this would necessitate purchase of locally made vehicles of types already
used by Government departments. Thus, procurement would be by local compe-
titive bidding under existing procedures which are satisfactory. Motorcycles
and cycles (US$0.3 M) would be purchased by individual staff according to
their preference.
4.06      Scientific equipment for research stations (US$0.2 M) would in-
volve small contracts from various suppliers of specialized items. Inter-
national competitive bidding would, therefore, not be practical. As most of
the world's leading manufacturers of scientific equipment are represented in
India, are competitive, and could offer local maintenance service, GOA's
established local procurement procedures utilizing competitive bidding where
appropriate would be employed. Orders for purchase of minor equipment and
furniture (US$0.6 M) would be bulked wherever possible and purchased follow-
ing similar competitive bidding procedures, except where valued at less than
US$50,000, when they would be purchased by prudent shopping through normal
trade channels. Assurances were obtained in accordance with the above.
4.07      Assurances were given that consultants employed (US$0.1 M) would
have qualifications, experience, and terms and conditions of employment that
would be mutually agreed between GOA and the Association. The balance of
project costs (US$9.1 M) consists of staff training (US$1.3 M), incremental
salaries and traveling allowances (US$3.5 M), and operating costs (US$4.3 M)
not involving procurement.
4.08      Assurances were given that for contracts in excess of US$100,000,
draft tender invitations and bid documents would be submitted for IDA review
before issue.
D. Disbursements and Audit
4.09      There would be no disbursements against tubewells and pumpsets
purchased by farmers, under the complementary groundwater program, nor against
incremental operating costs and salaries. Disbursements for other components
under the Credit would extend over five years for:
(a) 100% of foreign expenditures for overseas training and 80%
of expenditures for local training and consultancy services;
(b) 80% of cost of civil works and locally procured equipment
and vehicles; and
(c) 45% of salaries of incremental staff.



- 17 -
4.10      Disbursements against expenditures for locally procured equipment
costing Rs 20,000 or less, and payments under civil works contracts for one
or more progress payment not exceeding Rs 50,000 would be against a certifi-
cate of expenditure, the documentation for which would not be submitted for
review but would be retained by GOA. It would be available for inspection
by IDA during the course of project review missions. Disbursements against
all other items would be fully documented. Certificates of expenditure would
be audited at least once every six months and a report submitted to IDA
promptly thereafter. Suitable assurances were given. A schedule of esti-
mated disbursements is in Annex 12, Table 1.
4.11      Assurances were given that all institutions using project funds
would keep separate accounts of expenditures made under the project to be
audited annually, for each fiscal year, in accordance with sound auditing
principles consistently applied by independent and qualified auditors.
Audited accounts would be submitted to IDA not later than nine months after
the end of each such year.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
5.01      Overall responsibility for implementation of the reorganized
extension program, the program of adaptive trials and the groundwater pro-
grams would be with the Agricultural Production Commissioner (APC), through
the Secretaries of the Departments of Agriculture and Irrigation. Respon-
sibility for research at the commodity-oriented research stations would be
with the Director of Research, Assam Agricultural University. The APC would
be assisted by a project manager at Deputy Secretary level or above. Such a
manager has been appointed; he would work exclusively for the project and
project related matters. He would be responsible, through the APC, for
disbursement of and accounting for project funds and submission of periodic
progress reports in a form acceptable to IDA. Development agencies outside the
Departments of Agriculture and Irrigation and AAU, in particular the lending
banks and ARDC, would be coordinated through regular meetings at State and
district levels (Paras 5.05 - 5.07). To ensure efficient implementation of the
buildings component of the project (other than for AAU, which would be respon-
sible for its own building program), a Special Project Implementation unit
would be established by November 30, 1977, within the Public Works Department
(PWD) under a suitably qualified engineer; suitable assurances were given.
A buildings implementation schedule is in Annex 12, Table 3.
5.02      Extension.  A full time additional Director of Agriculture (ADA)
has been appointed and is responsible, under the Director of Agriculture, for
day to day administration and control of (a) the extension program and (b)
through a special adaptive trials cell to be set up at AAU (para 5.03), also
for the adaptive trials program. He would be assisted by a team of four senior
SMS who should be responsible for, inter alia, development of extension training
programs, arranging for staff to be sent to training institutions both within



- 18 -
and outside Assam, and review of extension recommendations. He would also be
assisted by two Zonal Joint Directors of Agriculture (JDA) and a small support
staff. This directorate would be responsible, inter alia, for: recruitment
and training of staff, budgeting and procurement, and supplying information
required by the APC for progress reports.
5.03      FTS.  The Department of Agriculture would be responsible for the
adaptive trials program at FTS. A special cell would be set up under a Joint
Director Agriculture (JDA) (Adaptive Trials) who would be directly responsible
to the Additional Director. Assurances were given that a full-time JDA (Adap-
tive Trials) would be appointed by December 31, 1977, or such later date as
may be agreed by the Association. The cell would be located at AAU, Jorhat,
and would comprise a production economist, statistician and agricultural
engineer. The cell, with technical backup from AAU, would be responsible for
overseeing work on the nine FTS through station heads, and would provide an
essential link between AAU and FTS. FTS station heads would be of the rank of
Deputy Director of Agriculture. Staff for FTS would be seconded to DOA from
AAU; this would ensure that MAU staff would spend substantial periods working
in the field.
5.04      AAU Research.  The Director of Research would be responsible for
execution of the research program at AAU and commodity stations and for
technical input into FTS. He would also be responsible for preparation and
submission of financial estimates to the APC (as funds contributed by GOA
for research are channeled through the Department of Agriculture), recruit-
ment of staff (including provision of staff for FTS), construction, procure-
ment, disbursement of and accounting for project funds and preparation of
progress reports to be submitted to IDA., through the APC. He would be
assisted by two senior scientists to be appointed under the project (pro-
posed TORs are in Annex 3, Attachment 3) who would be responsible for day-
to-day administrative and technical matters on the two main commodity research
stations and one substation to be strengthened under the project. Assurances
were given that GOA would cause AAU to employ a senior and experienced research
scientist by December 31, 1977, to assist in the formuiation of research
proposals and organization and management of research programs (Terms of
reference are in Annex 3, Attachment 2).
Cooperation Between Agriculture and Research
5.05      As neither extension nor research can function effectively without
close coordination, the relationship would be enhanced through regular meet-
ings at State and district levels, and through close working relationships at
district level. Technical committees would be set up at district level to
review and formulate FTS work programs, and extension recommendations for
passing on to VLEW at fortnightly training sessions (such committees have been
set up in the three districts in which the extension program is now opera-
tional). When formulated, these programs and recommendations would be sub-
mitted to a State level Technical committee, to be set up by November 30,
1977, and to be chaired by the Director of Agriculture. It would comprise
the head of the adaptive trials cell (as secretary), Director of Research,

Director of Extension Education (AAU), heads of the University Departments,

It would also assess input and credit supply requirements associated with
extension recommendations which would be passed to the appropriate supply
agency through the APC (para 5.08). Assurances were given that these state

Regarding research programs at AAU drawn up by the Director of Research, these
are currently vetted by a Research Advisory Committee. This committee will
shortly be replaced by a Statutory Research Council with wider representa-
tion, in particular from the DOA.

Project Coordination
5.08      A Project Coordinating Committee chaired by the Chief Secretary or
APC would be set up by November 30, 1977, to ensure cooperation among various
Government organizations, AAU and the banks. It would meet at least twice a

ensure that problems of credit, marketing and input supplies were given due
attention. The committee would include the Vice-Chancellor of the University,
the Secretaries of Agriculture, Irrigation and Cooperation, Registrar of
Cooperatives, Director Research (AAU), Director of Agriculture, Additional
Director of Agriculture, and, as required, representatives of the Assam
Agro-Industries Development Corporation, Fertilizer Corporation of India,
Assam Seed Corporation, Assam State Cooperative Consumer and Marketing
Federation, the Food Corporation of India, ARDC and concerned banks.
Credit
5.09      The cooperative credit structure, for both short and long term credit
is weak. This has largely been caused by lack of demand and a reluctance on
the part of the now defunct primaary cooperative credit societies to collect
outstanding payments. There have been several suggestions for strengthening
cooperative credit over the past few years, based largely on studies carried
out by the RBI. But several of the recommendations contained in these studies
have yet to be put into effect. Assurances were obtained that GOA would (i)
review, in consultation with RBT, RBI's past studies for strengthening the
cooperative credit structure in Assam, in particular, measures to improve the
collection of overdue debts, and would draw up by March 31, 1978, a time
schedule for the implementation of the recommendations arising from such
review, and (ii) thereafter, implement such recommendations accordingly. In
the case of long term credit, required particularly for implementation of
groundwater development, ARDC refinancing of lending by commercial banks to
farmers has proved satisfactory. Under the project this arrangement would
continue.
Monitoring and Evaluation
5.10      Monitoring (and reporting) of physical and financial inputs would
be the responsibility of the Secretary of the concerned Deparment or AAU as
appropriate. As discussed in para 3.16, to provide a measure of the degree of
achievement of project goals, as well as to generate data concerning the
performance of project organizations, this data needs to be supplemented with
further monitoring and evaluation on a sample basis by an external agency.
This requires continuous data gathering and, for this purpose, an evaluation
unit would be set up at headquarters under a Senior Statistician and would be
supported by small units at district level, each staffed by two or three field
data collectors. In turn, this ongoing evaluation would be supplemented with



- 21 -
in depth ad-hoc studies. Funds have been provided for the Secretary
Agriculture to commission outside agencies to help with evaluation and to
carry out ad hoc studies, as required. Agencies suitably qualified to do
this would include the already established Agro-Economic Research Center
for North East India (Jorhat) or the Department of Agricultural Economics
and Farm Management (AAU). Because of the need for uniform procedures for
monitoring and evaluation of the proposed project and of similar projects
being undertaken in other States, GOI, concerned States and IDA are currently
working out details and it is expected that these would be completed by July
1977. A summary of the draft report is in Annex 5. Assurances were given that
GOA would carry out continuous monitoring and evaluation of the project in a
manner to be mutually agreed with IDA by December 31, 1977. Results of such
monitoring and evaluation would be sent to IDA at least once a year.
VI. FARMERS BENEFITS
Farmers Benefits from Extension
6.01      As discused in para 2.17, the extension methodology would start
with recommendations to farmers that can increase yields with little or no
cash input and progressing, only as the farmers' ability and cash surplus
rises, to more costly means of production. Initial emphasis would be given
to low-cost improvements in cultural practices. Once these have improved, ex-
tension packages would be developed emphasizing low use of purchased inputs;
the first inputs promoted would be improved or high-yielding varieties and
seed treatment, both relatively cheap and capable of giving marked yield
improvements. This would be followed with low fertilizer doses and would
build up until routine use of pesticides and higher doses of fertilizer would
be recommended. In practice, details of extension recommendations would vary
between regions and farmers would adopt recommendations at very different
rates. Because of the uncertainty of the rate at which farmers would adopt
these practices, it is not possible to determine an aggregated increase in
production for the project. However, based on farmer trials in Assam the
following cumulative yield increases might be expected (details are in Annex
13) as farmers progress from one technology package to another:
--   about a 25% gain from improved cultural practices;
--   a further 20% gain from the adoption of improved
(or high yielding) varieties;
--   a fertilizer response ratio ranging from 5:1 on rainfed
crops to 10:1 on some irrigated crops (provided farmer
is already using HYV and improved cultural practices);
--   a further 15% gain from routine use of pesticides.



- 22 -
These yield increases are less than those reported by farmers following exten-
sion recommendations in Nowgong District in Assam, where the reorganized ex-
tension service has been operating for two crop seasons. In the IDA-financed
Chambal and Rajasthan Command Area Development Projects, provisional figures
indicate that over a three year period, yields of rice increased by an average
of 60%, wheat by 80% and those of mustard and gram more than doubled as a
result of improved extension of the type this project would provide. While
these are largely irrigated areas, it illustrates the magnitude of responses
actually achieved under good conditions.
Farmers Benefits from Investment in STW
6.02      Four farm models have been prepared (details in Annex 13) to examine
the financial viability of investing in a STW in Assam. The models assume
irrigation by purchase of a 3.5 hap STW at Rs. 7,500. It is expected that those
investing in a STW would be the mnore progressive farmers, who, if they did not
purchase a STW, would follow many of the extension recommendations for rainfed
farming. Benefits of investment in irrigation are, therefore, compared with
benefits that would be expected by the same (progressive) farmer under rainfed
conditions. Based on current crop and input prices, all irrigated models show
a doubling of cash income by full development; however, benefits under rainfed
conditions would also be considerable.  The opportunity cost (benefit stream
under rainfed conditions) has, therefore, been deducted from the benefit stream
under irrigation. Rates of return so calculated show that investment in STW
is financially attractive on a f].ood-free farm of 2 ha or over (even with a
25% reduction in the benefit stream returns vary from 36% to over 100%). How-
ever, on flood prone land farmers with less than about 3 ha would be finan-
cially better off without a STW. Criteria for sinking of STW are prescribed
by ARDC and are satisfactory.
VII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
7.01      Financial.  Total costs of extension and research over the five-year
project development period amount to about US$12.3 M or US$12 per benefitting
farm family -- about US$2.2 per capita -- (assuming I M farm families benefit,
Para 7.04). Incremental costs tc be met from GOA budget sources, following
project completion, would be about US$1.5 M per annum, or about US$1.5 per
benefitting farm family per annum. This additional requirement compares
with GOA present budgetary allocation to agriculture of about US$48 M equiv-
alent annually and would be met without difficulty. Incremental costs would
be partly recovered through levies on incremental paddy and other agricultural
produce entering commercial markets.
7.02      Economic (Extension & Research).  The principal economic benefit
of the project would be an increase in agricultural production, principally
foodgrains. Attributing a precise level of benefits to this type of proj-
act, however, is difficult since the expected benefits of improved agricul-
tural practices, which also require additional inputs, cannot be attributed
solely to extension and research. Moreover, there is not enough experience
in Assam to estimate acceptance rates with any degree of accuracy. However,



- 23 -
there is no doubt that the substantial improvement in the efficiency of
utilization of existing resources which would be brought about by the proj-
ect, would generate a high rate of return. It would be reasonable to expect
that by year 7 production of rice would increase by about 43,000 tons state-
wide, equivalent to an average yield increase of 20 kg/ha, or less than 2%
over the "without project" situation. Given such increases, the economic rate
of return would be 50%. Details are in Annex 14. Experience with reorganized
extension services in other States of India suggests that there would be
little risk that yields would not increase by an average of at least 20 kg/ha
as a result of the project, since this is well below gains recorded in farmer
trials in Assam and is less than gains reported by responding farmers in
Nowgong District, where the reorganized extension service has been in ope-
ration for two crop seasons (para 6.01). The high rate of return should,
however, be treated with caution since, as improved agricultural practices
spread, further investments in infrastructure and services would become
necessary.
7.03      Economic (Investment in STW).  Details of assumptions for calculating
the economic return to investment in a STW are in Annex 13. In brief, models
are as in Para 6.02, but using economic prices, family labor shadow priced at
Rs 3 per manday and investment costs charged at the time they are incurred.
Taxes have been excluded. The opportunity cost (benefits stream under rainfed
conditions) has again been deducted. Rates of return vary between 40% and
80%, showing that investment in STW is economically sound.
7.04      Number of Beneficiaries.  Experience in the Chambal and Rajasthan
Canal Command Area Development Projects indicates that after two years, more
than 50% of farm families followed some or most of the extension recommenda-
tions. Based on this assumption, the project could directly benefit about
200,000 contact farmers and more than 1 M of Assam's 1.9 M farm families
within the project period. Ultimately most of Assam's farming population are
expected to benefit. Only about 15,000 farm families would benefit from the
complementary STW program and these would mostly be large farmers. However,
the groundwater development program, including the technology development
program would lay the base for future development of a larger scale ground-
water program, in particular bringing irrigation within the means of even the
smallest farmers.
7.05      Social.  Past agricultural development strategy has aimed at
achieving production increases mainly through high doses of inputs. This
either requires credit which more often than not, is not available to small
farmers, or cash, also not available; consequently larger farmers have bene-
fitted most from past programs. The reorganized extension service would
focus first on recommendations primarily involving more labor -- an asset
most small farmers have in abundance. The project thus provides a unique
opportunity for small and marginal farmers to benefit. Aside from generating
high economic benefits, the extension and research components of the project
would, therefore, have a considerable beneficial impact on marginal and small
farmers, now living close to the poverty line.



- 24 -
