INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
The Present Agricultural Situation and Potential
for Development 1/
A. Background
1.        The most important crop in Assam is rice.  In 1975/76 it constituted
some 80% of the total area under annual crops in the state. Total production,
areas and average yields for main annual crops are shown below:
Crop          Production            Area /a       Yield
('000 tons)        ('000 ha)      (tons/ha)
Rice             2,290             2,241           1.02
Wheat               68                57           1.19
Pulses              36                91           0.40
Oilseeds            71               179           0.40
Jute and mesta    162                103           1.57
Sugar cane       1,589                41          38.76
/a  With a total area under annual crops of about 2.7 million
hectares and a net sown area of about 2.2 million hectares,
the average cropping intensity is about 120%.
Source: Department of Economics and Statistics.
These crops are almost totally confined to the plains, which consist of a
strip approximately 6aO km by 75 km lying in the Brahmaputra Valley and an
area of about 5000 km in Cachar District in the south of the state and
separated from the Brahmaputra Valley by the Cachar Hills. Ninety-seven per-
cent of Assam's population is concentrated in these valley areas (see Table 1)
where over 95% of the annual crops are grown.
2.        Climate.  The summer rainfall and temperatures in the plains of
Assam are particularly favorable for rainfed crop production. The 75% prob-
able monthly rainfall exceeds the upper limit of rice evapotranspiration from
the onset of pre-monsoon showers in early May until the end of October.
During this period average daily temperatures range between 22 and 32 0C.  Two
1/   Further details are given in India:  Eastern Region Foodgrains
Production Review (Assam), Report No. 1175-IN.



ANNEX I
Page 2
rainfed rice crops are theoretically possible, and conditions are also well
suited to the production of jui:e. Relatively cool and humid conditions during
the rest of the year ensure residual moisture is only slowly depleted so that
rabi crops of wheat, pulses and oilseeds can be grown. Rabi rice is also
possible, provided supplementary irrigation is available and cold-tolerant
varieties used. Representative monthly rainfall and temperature data are
given in Table 2.
3.        Soils.  The soils of the plains areas are alluvial and, especially
in the flood-prone areas which receive annual deposits of silt, relatively
fertile. The new alluvial soils that cover the greater part of the Brahma-
putra Valley are predominantly sandy or loamy soils of neutral reaction having
moderate levels of available phosphorus and potash, and can be worked with
draught animals even in the dry season. The older alluvial soils on the south
of the valley and in upper Assam are somewhat acidic, poorer in available
phosphorus and, having a heavier texture, cannot be ploughed when dry using
local draught animals. The motntain valley alluvial soils of Cachar district
are more acidic than the other two groups, are intermediate in texture and
have a nutrient content similar to the alluvial soils. There are no signi-
ficant salinity problems and drainage is adequate except in a few low-lying
areas. Extensive reserves of groundwater exist below much of the plains
areas, often at a depth of less than 5 meters (Annex 4).
4.        Floods.  Despite protection works, flooding is a recurring feature
of the Brahmaputra Valley during the peak monsoon period of mid-June to mid-
August. The area of crops damaged varies from year to year but usually aver-
ages above 200,000 ha (about 8% of the total area under crops). It is
estimated that some 400,000 ha of land area are at risk from flooding each
year. Floods usually rise and fall rapidly and unpredictably. Under such
circumstances, floating rice varieties are of little help, and farmers
on these flood-prone lands are usually forced to delay planting until late
in the monsoon to avoid flood damage.
5.        Landholdings.  The average holding is below 1.5 ha and 90% of far-
mers operate less than 3 ha. Moreover, the distribution of land is uneven
as shown below:
Size of holding in ha      % of holdings      % of cultivated land
Below 0.5                     33                     6
0.5 - 1.0                    24                     12
1.0 - 2.0                    24                     24
2.0 - 3.0                    10                     17
3.0 - 5.0                      7                    18
Over  5.0                      2                    23
100                   100
Over 40% of land is contained in the small number of holdings over 3 ha.
However, since these farmers are likely to have more resources to invest



ANNEX 1
Page 3
and better access to inputs, the larger holdings can make a substantial con-
tribution to increased foodgrains production. Official statistics suggest
that only some 15% of land is rented and a negligible proportion sharecropped.
There are indications, however, that sharecropping is more prevalent and may
involve up to 20% of farmers.
B. Present Practices
Major Crops and Cropping Seasons
6.        There are three rice growing seasons in Assam.  The main rice crop
(sali) is transplanted in July and harvested in November or December. It
receives the full monsoon rain. Second is the ahu crop, traditionally direct
seeded at the onset of pre-monsoon showers in March or April and harvested in
June or July. Third is the rabi boro crop, of only minor importance. It is
transplanted into low-lying areas from January to March, harvested in May,
and usually receives some irrigation by traditional means. As the timings of
the three crops overlap, farmers concentrate on the sali crop when water supply
is most assured. The following table shows the relative importance of the
three rice crops (1975/76):
Crop          Production        Area           Yield
('000 tons)    ('000 ha)       (tons/ha)
Ahu (pre-monsoon)        478            636           0.75
Sali (monsoon)         1,767          1,566           1.13
Boro (rabi season)        45             39           1.15
Source: Department of Economics and Statistics
7.        Wheat, pulses and oilseeds are grown on residual moisture as rabi
crops (Table 3). They are sown November to January after harvest of sali
rice. Present traditional sali varieties mature too late to allow rabi
crops to be planted at the optimum time; hence, rabi crops may suffer water
stress which can severely limit yield. The wheat area has increased from a
few hundred hectares ten years ago to about 60,000 ha, at the expense of the
traditional rabi oilseed crops, rape and mustard.
8.        Other major crops are jute and sugar cane.  Jute is sown principally
on flood-prone land in April and harvested in July. Sugar cane is grown in
flood-free areas, planted with the monsoon, harvested after 12 months, and
generally ratooned twice.
Standard of husbandry
9.        Crop husbandry practices are traditional.  Land is prepared by time-
consuming methods using numerous shallow passes of the traditional chisel



ANNEX I
Page 4
(desi) plough, alternating with laddering. For dry-sown crops, clods are
frequently broken by hand using long-handled mallets. Incorporation into the
soil of perennial weeds is poor and land levelling for paddy and irrigated
crops is uneven. Rainfed fields are only lightly bunded. No drainage channels
are provided for rainfed or irrigated fields and the efficiency of water uti-
lization on irrigated areas is low. Except for wheat and some 10% of the rice
area, improved varieties are seldom used.
10.       Direct-seeded crops, whether ahu paddy or rabi wheat, oilseeds or
pulses, are normally broadcast. Annual weeds are numerous in these crops and,
due to the incidence of water stress, highly damaging. Since they are not
line-sown, control measures are confined to occasional hand-pulling of weeds
and, in the case of ahu paddy, to cross harrowing which severely disturbs
both crop and weeds. Yield losses due to weed competition are considerable.
For high-yielding shorter statured rice varieties, which are less tolerant
of harrowing, losses are even greater. Plant populations for rabi crops and
direct-seeded rice are often sub-optimal, and transplanted paddy planting
points are often too widely spaced with too many seedlings per hole. Sub-
optimal plant populations enhance weed competition and further reduce yields.
11.       Harvesting, threshing and storage practices are also traditional.
The climate of Assam poses special problems for the ahu rice crop, which is
harvested at the height of the monsoon, and to a lesser extent for wheat.
Safe storage of these crops is often impossible after drying in damp and cloudy
conditions, and spoilage is common. Saved seed often shows poor germination
for the same reason, although many farmers appear to have accepted a recent
Department of Agriculture recommendation to store saved seed in polythene
bags, with a period of sun-drying every three months.
12.       The benefits of organic manuring are well known to farmers but the
average FYM application rate appears to be only about 1 ton/ha. This is
low in relation to official statistics for the number of domestic animals,
suggesting that it would not be difficult to double this dose. Collected
manure is frequently stored in the open where it is leached by rains before
spreading.
Draught power, implements and input use
13.       Local bullocks are small and capable of a sustained draught of only
about 35 kg per pair. A 3-inch iron mouldboard plough has been developed
by the Assam Agricultural University and gives some soil inversion with a
better rate of work than the desi plough, but less than 1% of the farmers
own one. Bullock-drawn or hand-operated row seeders are virtually non-
existent, and a recent survey shows only one tractor per 5,500 hectares of
cropping. Powered sprayers and threshers are even less numerous.
14.       Use of purchased inputs in Assam is the lowest in India.  Fertilizer
use in 1974/75 is estimated at only some 5,000 tons nutrients (an average of
about 2-3 kg/ha), after reaching> a peak in 1972/73 (see table below). Only
about 10% of rice is of improved local or high-yielding varieties. Seed



ANNEX 1
Page  5
sales (excluding potatoes and sugar cane setts) in 1974/75 totalled about
5,000 tons and most saved seed is sown without fungicide dressing.  Pesticides
are used occasionally on rice and jute but seldom on wheat, pulses, oilseeds
or sugar cane. Only 4000 farmers used institutional production credit in 1974/
75. Cash resources available to the average farmer for financing production
are low. A sample survey by the Assam Agricultural University (AMU) showed
that traditional farmers operating up to 3 hectares spent less than Rs 50 per
farm per year.
Fertilizer Consumption in Agriculture in Assam 1969/70-1974/75
(nutrient tons)
N          P025        K20         Total
1969/70       3,440         1,880        1,150       6,470
1970/71       4,120         1,330        1,000       6,450

1974/75       3,780           319          434       4,533
15.       The consequences of these unchanging traditional agricultural prac-
tices and the limited use of modern technologies (except on wheat) can be
seen in Table 3. Except for wheat, yields show no significant improvement
over the past 11 years (1965/66-1975/76).
C. Production Potential and Constraints
Production Potential
16.       There are three ways in which foodgrain production might be in-
creased - by increasing the area under cultivation, by raising crop yields
or by raising cropping intensity.    ,
Increasing the area under cultivation
17.       There is little scope for bringing new land under foodgrain culti-
vation. Although only 28% of the geographical area is devoted to annual crop-
ping, most of the remainder is either unsuitable due to slope or rockiness
(33%), already under perennial crops such as tea (2%), or scheduled as forest
reserves (27%). Only some 180,000 ha (2%) are classified as cultivable waste.
Even if this area could be brought under cultivation it would add only 8% to
the present area available for annual cropping.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
Increasing crop yields
18.       There is ample scope for increasing crop yields.  In supervised
farmer trials yields from HYV sali rice with high fertilizer doses averaged
3.0 to 3.5 tons rice/ha, about three times the present state average, and for
HYV ahu rice without fertilizer farmers yields averaged 2.1 tons/ha, almost
double the present state average. In weed control trials on sali rice, yields
were more than double the present state average. Fertilizer trials on wheat
have shown 40% yield increases over the present state average.
Increasing cropping intensity
19.       The theoretical scope for raising cropping intensity is considerable
(see para 2). Since most farmers take a full sali rice crop, increasing crop-
ping intensity in practice means growing larger areas of ahu rice and/or rabi
crops. Supervised trials have demonstrated the possibility of triple rainfed
cropping by small farmers in the Jorhat area, with double-cropping on flood-
prone areas. Farmers in a number of areas are already achieving a cropping
intensity of some 170%.
20.       Before a statewide strategy to increase cropping intensity in rain-
fed areas can be formulated, a balance between ahu and rabi seasons must be
determined. Ahu cropping, despite the availability of good quick-maturing
rice varieties, will always be subject to variable pre-monsoon showers and
therefore involves some risk. To take full advantage of early rainfall, rapid
seedbed preparation is necessary and land needs to be prepared when dry; this
is constrained by existing draught power and implements. Moreover, ahu rice
matures at the height of the monsoon causing drying and storage problems. The
desirability of generating a surplus of foodgrains at that time of year is
therefore questionable. For these reasons, major emphasis on increasing ahu
intensity may only be justified in areas where flooding makes it difficult to
raise intensity at any other time of the year.
21.       Increased rabi cropping intensity, since it relies on residual
moisture, does not require soil to be ploughed when dry. It is also easier
for the farmer to assess the risk of subsequent water shortage (in some areas
water may remain within a few centimeters of the surface throughout the year
by capilliary rise from the water table). Planting of rabi crops should be
earlier than at present if establishment and yields are to be more reliable.
This, in turn, would require quicker land preparation or, preferably, an ear-
lier sali harvest. Traditional sali varieties are photoperiod sensitive so
earlier planting will have little effect. The quicker-maturing dwarf HYVs,
while insensitive to photoperiod, are more likely to suffer from the excessive
water depths of the sali season even in non-flood-prone areas. Nonetheless,
these problems are minor compared with those of ahu rice. Increased rabi
cropping is the most practical means of increasing overall cropping intensity.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
22. Irrigation (Annex 4)
Production constraints
23.       These fall under five major headings:
(i) Bad husbandry practices. These include inadequate weed
control, incorrect crop spacing, incorrect timing of
operations to take advantage of favorable conditions
(usually due to poor management of crop rotations),
insufficient organic manuring and failure to exclude
grazing animals from crops. All are items the farmer
himself can remedy at low cash cost and with more effi-
cient use of family labor.
(ii) Constraints over which the farmer has no direct control.
These include flooding, which compels him to plant at
times of increased drought risk, and the heavy rainfall
and low effective solar radiation of the monsoon season
which predispose the ahu crop to post-harvest spoilage
and limit the growth of the sali crop. Variability of
rainfall, especially of pre-monsoon showers, is a
further constriant to rainfed yields and, through its
interaction with ease of land preparation, to cropping
intensity. As a source of risk, it appears to deter many
farmers from attempting to grow any rainfed crop outside
the monsoon.
(iii) Inadequate draught power and implements. Slow land prep-
aration by poor draught animals with traditional imple-
ments both limits cropping intensity and delays the time
of crop planting. Improved implements from elsewhere in
India are usually too heavy for local cattle, and devel-
opment of miniaturized versions which would increase the
rate of work of Assamese cattle has been slow. Programs
for improvement of rearing practices and upgrading of
stock are at an early stage. Line sowing, which permits
weed control by inter-row cultivation, although possible
with traditional equipment, is limited by the lack of
specialized seeders. Few farmers have any equipment
for inter-row weeding apart from the traditional hoe.
(iv) Non-adoption of modern inputs. High-yielding or local
improved varieties, fertilizers and pesticides have gener-
ally been promoted in the past as a complete package, the
adoption of which involved a cash outlay of at least Rs 500
per hectare. Compared with a total annual cash investment
as low as Rs 50 per farm, cost alone has been a major
obstacle to their adoption. Few farmers risk borrowing the
cash and fewer still can provide it from their own resources.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
Another major disincentive to adoption has been the low
general standard of husbandry (see (i) above). Under such
circumstances, many farmers have been unable to obtain the
potential benefit of the high-input package, have not earned
enough to cover the increased costs and to guarantee adequate
profits, and have reverted to traditional practices.
(v) Institutional factors. The principal institution-related
constraint has been the failure of extension to raise basic
husbandry standards to the level where farmers could obtain
real benefits from modern inputs. The institutions concerned
with input supply and seasonal credit have been inadequate.
However, with the present low demand for inputs and/or un-
willingness to borrow money to finance HYV production, the
defects of these institutions are not an immediate constraint
to production. Nor will they be for several years as improved
technologies are not advocated as the initial means of raising
production.

ANNEX 2
Page 1
INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Extension and Training
A. Introduction
1.        The Government of Assam (GOA) has recognized that a modern profes-
sional extension service can rapidly and inexpensively help Assam's two mil-
lion farm families raise yields and incomes. As indicated in Annex 1, the
potential for such increases in Assam is great. Statewide production in-
creases of about 7% p.a. over the next 6-7 years are feasible. That farmers
do respond to changes which clearly increase their standards of living has
been demonstrated by both the growing use of high yielding varieties and the
increasing importance of wheat. But the spread of new technology has been
slow and limited. Moreover, the standards of cultivation of many farmers are
so low that without improvement in basic management practices they cannot reap
the full benefits of modern technology. An efficient extension service is
needed to help farmers improve practices, adopt new technology and increase
their incomes.
2.        GOA has, therefore, decided to reorganize its extension service
to meet this task. GOA will rely primarily on existing staff and facilities.
Nevertheless additional staff, training programs, vehicles, housing and other
facilities will be needed. This project would provide the support required.
B. Background
Rural Administration
3.        Assam can be divided administratively and topographically into
plain and hill areas. There are 8 administrative districts (9 agricultural)
in the plains areas and 2 in hilly areas. 95% of the State's foodgrain is
produced in the plains districts. Because of significant administrative dif-
ferences between the hills and the plains, GOA has decided to confine the
reorganization of extension to plains districts. This description of the
present system is, therefore, confined to these areas. Each district is fur-
ther subdivided into two or three administrative units called subdivisions.
There are 20 administrative subdivisions (21 agricultural) in the plains dis-
tricts each further subdivided into 3 to 10 blocks, with 120 blocks in all.
A new administrative unit, the Gaon Panchayat (GP), was established by GOA in
1973. There are about 660 such units or about 5-6 in each block.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
Present Extension Services
4.        At present, field level extension work is the primary responsibi-
lity of the Village Level Worker (VLW), a multipurpose worker employed by the
Department of Community Development and Panchayats and directly responsible
to that Department's representative at the block level, the Block Development
Officer (BDO). The BDO is responsible to the District Deputy Commissioner,
the chief administrative officer at the district level. The Deputy Commis-
sioner's responsibilities cover all matters of government in his district.
There are normally 10 VLW per block, giving a total of about 1,200 in the
plains area, or an average of one VLW per 1,700 farm families. The VLW re-
ceives technical guidance from the Agricultural Extension Officer (AEO), an
employee of the Department of Agriculture. Both VLW and AEO, however, are
administratively responsible to BDO. There are about two AEO per block. AEO
are responsible on matters of substance to the Sub-divisional Agricultural
Officer (SDAO) (one per subdivision) who in turn reports to the District
Agricultural Officer (DAO) (one per district). DAO is backed up by a number
of Subject Matter Specialists (SMS) (normally at least one each for agronomy,
plant protection and horticulture), District Agricultural Information Officers
and other specialists. In the plains districts there are about 64 such staff.
5.        Most DAO in the plains districts report to one of two zonal Joint
Directors of Agriculture (JDA). One JDA covers the eastern end of the Brahma-
putra Valley and is stationed at Jorhat; the other covers the western end and
is stationed in Gauhati. The exception is the DAO of the IADP district Cachar,
who reports to a special JDA responsible for the Intensive Program. These
three JDA, together with other JDA and Deputy Directors of Agriculture res-
ponsible for special crops (e.g. jute) or special agricultural programs (e.g.
multiple cropping), report to an Additional Director of Agriculture (General).
6.        In addition to staff outlined above, others have been posted in
support of special schemes in selected blocks. In plains districts there
are about 630 field level Agricultural Demonstrators with training similar
to that of VLW but employed directly by the Department of Agriculture. At
approximately the same administrative level and also employed by the Depart-
ment of Agriculture are about 170 Assistant Agricultural Inspectors. In
addition to the 210-220 AEO in the normal chain of command, there are an
additional 160 AEO or AEO-level staff associated with special schemes or
regulatory functions (para 25).
Present Training
7.        VLW receive a two-year pre-service training course at one of
two Gram Sevak (Village Worker) Training Centers (GSTC). One GSTC has 100
student places, the other 65. In addition to pre-service training, GSTC
also offer a one-year in-service training course. Courses taught cover
a wide range of subjects besides agriculture, in line with VLW's present
multipurpose role.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
8.        The Assam Agricultural University (AAU) offers pre-service and
limited in-service training for higher level extension personnel (see Annex 3
for a discussion of the AAU research program). Under an IDA-financed educa-
tion project (Credit 342-IN), University facilities are being expanded and
improved with staff trained both in India and abroad. Through its College of
Agriculture, the University offers training for B.Sc. and M.Sc. degrees.
Currently about 70-80 students receive B.Sc. degrees annually and 15 receive
M.Sc. degrees. The College of Veterinary Science provides Bachelor and Masters
degrees in Veterinary Science. The AAU Directorate of Extension Education
also has the capability of offering intensive short inservice courses to ex-
tension personnel. In practice these courses have been largely limited to
pre-Kharif and pre-Rabi season workshops of 2-3 days duration during which
the package of practices agreed upon by AAU and Department of Agriculture are
explained and discussed. Normally staff from the subdivision level and above
attend.
Present Problems
9.        The present extension service has serious problems which GOA is
determined to overcome. Perhaps most serious is the lack of a single line of
command for extension staff. With VLW under the control of another agency,
it is inevitable that non-agricultural tasks dominate his work. While agri-
cultural activities are supposed to consume 80% of his time, field studies
suggest only 20-30% is spent on agriculture. Even then activities are likely
to be confined to non-productive report writing and regulatory work, so that
the time spent helping farmers in the field is insignificant. Closely related
is the problem of diffusion of VLW responsibilities by designating him a mul-
tipurpose worker. Being responsible for virtually all aspects of rural de-
velopment, collection of statistics, regulatory work, etc., he becomes respon-
sible for virtually nothing. His responsibilities in one sphere become an
excuse to avoid responsibilities in another and vice versa. Strong incentives
exist for his doing only those tasks which are now most clearly defined and
supervised (e.g. writing reports).
10.       In the past, "speciaL schemes" based on particular crops or aspects
of agriculture were adopted in attempts to overcome the basic weaknesses of
the system. Over time a large number of such schemes have emerged. However,
they have only served to weaken the extension effort further by dissipating
efforts and obscuring the need for fundamental reform of the extension service.
11.       In addition to VLW efforts being spread thinly over many subjects,
they are also spread over too many farmers. Moreover, extension work is
neither systematically planned nor adequately supervised, and at all levels
extension staff lack the mobility necessary for effectively carrying out their
work.
12.       The training of extension staff is inadequate and often outdated.
Most extension staff from VLW up received their agricultural training more
than ten years ago. Many new developments in agriculture have passed them



ANNEX 2
Page 4
by. In-service training has been inadequate, sporadic, and often irrelevant.
Linkages with research have been weak, with the only contact being the semi-
annual, two-day workshop mentioned above; a regular flow of information
from the field to research and back again has not been established. Among
other problems, this lack of contact means that recommendations developed by
research and passed on to farmers by the extension service are often inappro-
priate both for farmers' needs and for their technical and financial abilities.
Recommendations usually focus on high levels of use of purchased inputs such
as fertilizer, rather than on improved cultivation practices (such as weeding)
which cost little, bring sure results, and in any case are necessary to use
purchased inputs effectively.
C. The Proposed Agricultural Extension System
13.       The project would support the reorganization of the extension service
in Assam. A well-trained, highly motivated, and technically competent single
organization would be created, making optimal use of existing staff. The
organization would be given the means to reach the majority of Assam's farmers
with relevant and practical technical guidance aimed at increasing production
and income. The available manpower, supplemented as required by additional
staff, has excellent potential to achieve results provided it is properly
reorganized to concentrate on extension per se, and is subjected to continuing
and frequent training. VLW would be redesignated as VLEW (Village Level Ex-
tension Workers) to reflect their exclusive extension role. The new extension
system would cover the plains districts of the entire State within three years.
14.       The crux of the reorganized extension service is the single line of
command from Director of Agriculture down to VLEW. In Assam this will be
achieved by transferring to the Department of Agriculture 80% of VLW already
working in Blocks under CD. This would leave about 2 VLW per Block with the
BDO to cover non-agricultural tasks. All field staff in "special schemes" and
a portion of other DOA administrative and regulatory staff will be redeployed
for general agricultural extension work, thus providing about 1660 VLEW. About
780 new VLEW posts would be established and filled over a three year period
with graduates from a revised intensive training program at the existing GSTC.
This would provide 2440 VLEW for Assam's nearly 2 million farm families, a
ratio of I VLEW for about 800 farm families. VLEW would devote their time
exclusively to agricultural extension work and would not be assigned regula-
tory or administrative work. Their work would be organized into a systematic
time-bound program of training and visits with close supervision. This system
was introduced on a pilot basis in one subdivision (covering over 9% of the
State's foodgrain area) in April 1976, with good reactions from both extension
staff and farmers. Many farmers are already reporting substantial production
gains from the adoption of practices as simple as line transplanting and weed-
ing. Two additional subdivisions will be covered soon.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
The Extension Methodology
15.       The extension technique is based on a systematic program of train-
ing full-time agricultural workers (VLEW) combined with frequent visits by
VLEW to farmers' fields. This system enables a VLEW to visit each group of
farmers once a fortnight after he has received intensive training in those
agricultural practices and recommendations which relate directly to farm
operations during that fortnight. The fixed-schedule of visits enables close
supervision.
16.       The organizational structure (see Chart 1) is based on the total
number of farm families and the number of families which one VLEW can reason-
ably expect to visit. In Assam where population density is high, a ratio of
one VLEW per 800 farm families will be adequate. The estimated 1.9 million
farm families of Assam would be divided into 2,440 groups of about 800 fami-
lies each, to which one VLEW would be allocated. Each unit, in turn, would be
divided into 8 groups of about 100 farm families each. Each of these groups
would be visited by the VLEW, on a fixed day of the week, once every two weeks.
Thus, a VLEW would cover 4 groups in one week and 4 groups the next. A VLEW
would spend the full day with the scheduled farmers' group; during the morning
hours he would visit their fields and, in the afternoon, hold an open meeting
in the village at a predetermined hour. One of the four remaining working
days each fortnight would be devoted to in-service training during which VLEW
learn recommended practices to be given to farmers during the following fort-
night and have an opportunity to bring farmers' problems to the attention of
trainers (para 29). One day in the following week VLEW would meet together
with their supervisor, the AEO, as a group so that points taught in the pre-
vious week's training season can be reinforced and problems encountered re-
solved. During the two remaining days VLEW would make visits to supervise
field trials, arrange any special extension activities, or make up visits
missed because of illness or holidays and complete office work. VLEW would
maintain a chart showing days for visiting each group, as well as the time
and place of the meeting. His supervisor, the AEO, would keep such charts
for all VLEW supervised by him. An example of a chart is shown below.
A TYPICAL TIMETABLE FOR A VLEW
MON  TUE  WED  THU   FRI  SAT  SUN    MON  TUE  WED  THU  FRI  SAT  SUN
EXT                                    EXT
1    2    3    4   TRA   VIS   H        5    6    7    8   AEO  VIS   H
1    =    Visit Farmers Group
TRA  =    Training
AEO  =    Meeting with AEO
EXT       Extra visits for checking field trials, office work,
VIS       make up visits due to holidays, illness, etc.
H    =    Holiday



ANNEX 2
Page 6
Concentration of Efforts
17.       Contact farmers.  To concentrate extension efforts and achieve a
visible impact on production, VLEW would concentrate on selected contact
farmers, since it is clearly not possible for each VLEW to reach all farmers
by direct contact. Under technical guidance from above and in consultation
with farmers themselves, VLEW would select about 10 contact farmers from
within each group. Contact farmers would be from all levels of village
society, including sharecroppers, and would be selected for their potential
influence and willingness to collaborate with extension workers. Changes
in contact farmers could be made, especially between seasons, if they were
ineffective or if they so desired. VLEW, during field visits, would make
special efforts to visit all contact farmers' fields, and these farmers, in
turn, would share the responsibility of organizing and attending afternoon
meetings and allowing fields to be used as demonstration plots and for field
trials.
18.       As extension efforts would be concentrated on selected farmers, so
also would VLEW concentrate on the most important crops; focussing mainly on
those practices which bring the best economic results and on making optimum
use of available resources. Initially, concentration would be on improving
agricultural management practices and would include better land and seedbed
preparation, improved nursery maintenance, use of good seed, seed treatment,
timely operations such as weeding and proper spacing of plants, etc. Thus
stress would be on improved management practices which are known to produce
sure and quick results. In adopting better practices farmers face little
risk, but will be required to work harder. Since most small farmers have
surplus labor but little cash this fits their financial situation well.
Moreover, farmers often cannot get full benefit from purchased inputs or more
costly investments (such as tubewells) until their practices have improved.
19.       Cultural practices requiring basic changes would first be recom-
mended for adoption on only a small part of the land. This would overcome
a natural reluctance to rapid change and allow results to be compared with
traditional practices. Once farmers' yields and incomes increase substantially,
confidence in VLEW will grow and farmers will become more receptive to further
advice. Simple improvements in cultural practices can be introduced into an
extension service quickly and without requiring an elaborate, time-consuming
screening and trial process. They will also allow the FTS to come into opera-
tion and start generating further relevant messages for the extension system.
Thus the extension service can rapidly make that initial impact which will
ensure its continued success. After improving management practices, farmer
incomes would be higher and they would be financially better able to adopt
more expensive (e.g. fertilizer) or more dramatic (e.g. new varieties or
cropping patterns) recommendations. As farmers move to this stage, however,
emphasis would be on how to use limited purchased inputs to the best advantage.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
Organizational and Technical Support
20.       To ensure adequate supervision and training of VLEW, it would be
necessary to strengthen technical and support staff. Table 1 shows the
number of staff required and available at various levels. Chart I shows
the general organizational structure. The number of VLEW posts would be
increased to about 2,440. Under the project 305 AEO would be drawn from
existing agricultural staff to provide field level supervision and technical
support. Each AEO would supervise about 8 VLEW and would join each one
at least once every two weeks during his visits to farmers. This would
enable him to visit every farmers' group at least twice each season. He
would check that VLEW visits and meetings were carried out on schedule and
would assist both VLEW and farmers in technical matters. He would also
assist VLEW in carrying out demonstrations and simple trials. Once every
two weeks he would meet his VLEW as a group to reinforce the lessons of the
previous week's training and suggest adjustments, if necessary. He would also
join VLEW in their fortnightly training sessions with SMS (para 29). This
close ongoing relationship between AEO and VLEW should relieve the latter of
the need for formal reporting. VLEW would be expected, however, to keep a
diary to be scrutinized and signed by any supervising officer visiting him.
21.       Staff supervising VLEW and AEO would know VLEW and AEO schedules of
visits, and would be able to find out quickly and easily whether visits were
being made as planned, not only by making surprise visits to areas in which
VLEW and AEO are scheduled on particular days, but also by questioning farmers
about visit days, asking the names of VLEW and AEO, messages learned, etc.
Experience in Assam has already shown that, once farmers become aware of the
visit schedule, they themselves become effective supervisors of the work of
VLEW and AEO.
22.       The Subdivisional Agricultural Officer (SDAO) supervises AEO and
VLEW under his jurisdiction and is in overall charge of the training pro-
grams. One SDAO can supervise effectively about 8 AEO. A number of Assam's
present administrative subdivisions are so large that an SDAO might have
several times this number of AEO. Moreover, they would contain too many VLEW
for one team of SMS to train effectively (para 23). Consequently six exist-
ing subdivisions will be further divided, establishing within them six new
agricultural subdivisions. Even then, most subdivisions would have more than
9 AEO. In such cases Additional SDAO (ASDAO) would be appointed to share SDAO
supervisory responsibilities. New posts would be created for SDAO of new
subdivisions and for ASDAO. The District Agricultural Officer (DAO) will be
responsible for the operation of the extension system in his district and
will supervise SDAO.
23.       Technical Support Staff.  To provide technical backup and support
at field level, two Subject Matter Specialists (SMS) (one specializing in
field crops and the other in plant protection) would be posted in each sub-
division. Thus two SMS would support and train about 90 VLEW. They would
spend about one-third of their time on field visits with AEO and VLEW,



ANNEX 2
Page 8
helping them solve technical problems and conducting and supervising field
trials. Each SMS would also spend about one-third of his time training
groups of VLEW once each fortnight. For such training VLEW in each subdivi-
sion would be divided into 2-4 groups of manageable size. The remainder of
the SMS time would be devoted to visits to and collaboration with local re-
search facilities (Field Trial Stations, etc.). In subdivisions in which the
shallow tubewell program is concentrated, an additional SMS (agronomist/water
management) would be posted. As it is not possible to provide more than
minimal technical support at subdivisional level, additional support would
be provided at each of the 9 districts, in the form of two senior and better-
trained SMS, probably concentrating on agronomy/water management and plant
protection. In conjunction with FTS staff, they would play a central role
in developing local recommendations and in passing them on to AEO and VLEW at
training sessions (paras 29-32) and would form a vital link between research
and extension. The existing District Agricultural Information Officer would
be responsible for administration of the training programs in the District,
coordinating participation of the subdivisional SMS, SDAO, District SMS, and
researchers in training activities and in organizing and supervising use of
audiovisual aids and equipment in the District.
24.       At headquarters level, the present Director of Agriculture would be
assisted by an Additional Director of Agricultural (Extension) (ADA). This
would be a new post with full time responsibility for supervising the exten-
sion service and ensuring its smooth operation. ADA (Extension) would be
assisted by two existing Zonal Joint Directors of Agriculture covering res-
pectively, the Eastern and Western Zones of the state. He would also receive
technical support from a team of four senior subject-matter specialists (one
each in crop production, crop protection, water management and farming sys-
tems). This specialist team would be responsible for technical and profes-
sional aspects of the service, including developing in-service training pro-
grams for extension personnel (particularly SMS), both within and outside
Assam, maintaining close contact with researchers in Assam, the rest of India
and other countries, and reviewing technical recommendations to be made.
Organizational Chart 2 shows the relationship between the extension service
and research institutions.

numbers of available staff and incremental staff required at each level:

Details of requirements by subdivision and by year are shown in Tables I
and 2. Salaries of additional staff required form part of total project

Salaries and Incentives
26.       Extension work is carried out under difficult conditions and pays
poorly. Salaries would not be increased; the project would, however,
provide for advanced increments to be offered on a yearly basis to 25% of
VLEW and other selected staff who have shown willingness, devotion and
results in their work. Provision of some housing, motorcycles and bicycles
(para 42-44) would be a considerable incentive to field staff. Adequate
allowances for travel and housing would be provided.
Demonstrations, Experiments and Field Trials
27.       Past use of field demonstrations in India's agricultural exten-
sion programs has been wide but with varying results. The project places
no special emphasis on a demonstration program, but will rather utilize
fields of contact farmers, where new practices would be demonstrated; in
addition, simple field trials would be carried out on experimental farms and
farmers' fields. These demonstrations would form an integral part of the work
of every VLEW and AEO, and would be supported by SMS, district specialists,
and research workers from the local FTS (Annex 3).
Evolution of Extension Recommendations
28.       Before each crop season, District Level Extension and Trials Com-
mittees, headed by the DAO and consisting of SMS, staff of the local Field
Trials Station and possibly selected farmers, would compile recommendations



ANNEX 2
Page 10
arising out of the field trials program in the area and from their own ex-
perience. These suggestions would be screened by a State Level Technical
Committee, chaired by Director of Agriculture, which would also consider
recommendations from specialist staff at extension headquarters, the Assam
Agricultural University and other sources. Those recommendations approved by
the State Level Technical Committee would be reviewed by the District Level
Committees to ensure that they are suited to local conditions, and modified
as necessary. Recommendations would then be formulated into a series of fort-
nightly impact points for the coming season. Through a parallel process the
program of field trials for the season would also be developed (para 27).
Chart 3 describes this process.
Inservice Training (see Chart 4)
(a) VLEW Training
29.       Continuous inservice training of VLEW is an essential part of the
new approach to extension. Each fortnight on a fixed day, VLEW would receive
intensive instruction on the few specific recommendations relevant to farming
operations in the next fortnight. This is the most critical of all the train-
ing programs, and efforts should be concentrated on making it effective. The
training would be practically oriented and would provide time for VLEW to carry
out specific recommendations, in the field and for him to practice how he would
approach farmers with these recommendations. VLEW will be given pamphlets,
summarizing recommendations, and samples or other visual aids as required. At
these sessions VLEW would also have an opportunity to raise and discuss prob-
lems encountered in previous weeks. When adequate SMS are available, this
training session would be conducted by SMS with the assistance of the relevant
AEO and SDAO. VLEW in each subdivision would be divided into groups of about
30 for this training; SMS may, as a temporary expedient, train AEO who in turn
would train VLEW. The DAO and District-level SMS and Agricultural Information
Officer would supervise and assist at these training sessions. Training ses-
sions would be held at Field Trial Stations if possible, otherwise at sub-
divisional or district level headquarters. Staff of the local Field Trial
Stations would participate.
30.       These fortnightly training sessions also provide an opportunity to
ensure coordination between the extension service and input supply and credit
agencies. VLEW will no longer be responsible for gathering statistics on
these matters, but from his and the AEO's contact with the farmers, he should
be able to point out supply constraints and inform input supply/credit agencies
about recommendations and what proportion of farmers are likely to follow them.
AEO and VLEW could also ascertain the credit/input supply position from these
agencies and report any problems noted by farmers in obtaining supplies. It
would not normally be necessary for representatives of the input supply and
credit agencies to attend more than one of these sessions a month and such
matters should normally not occupy more than one hour of the training session.
31.       Tn addition to the regular fortnightly training sessions there
would also be two pre-season training sessions a year for VLEW. These would



ANNEX 2
Page 11
last 2-3 days and be conducted at subdivisional or district headquarters, or
at Field Trial Stations, by district and subdivisional extension officers
and SMS, Field Trial Station staff and AAU staff as available. 1/ The object
of those sessions would be to provide VLEW with an overview of recommendations
for the season as a whole, enabling them to better understand the individual
fortnightly recommendations. The sessions would not only provide an opportu-
nity for VLEW to react to these recommendations, but also serve to emphasize
extension goals and priorities. An additional specialized training course of
about one week would be given each year covering one particular topic (e.g.
rice production, wheat production, soil identification and fertility, etc.).
Training would be by SMS with assistance from AAU and Headquarters specialists.
(b) AEO & Senior Staff
32.       Once a month, on a fixed day AEO would attend a one-day training/
planning session at subdivisional headquarters. The program would be con-
ducted by subdivisional level staff supplemented by district level specialists
and would cover the training program for the upcoming month. During this ses-
sion a detailed schedule of activities (training, demonstrations, etc.) would
be fixed.
33.       Two pre-season training sessions would be held annually at district
level for AEO and subdivisional level staff, prior to the pre-season training
of VLEW.  This course would be organized by the DAO and his staff following
the workshops during which the statewide extension recommendations were
developed and approved (see para 28).
34.       SMS play a critical role in providing training and technical support
to VLEW.  As few existing SMS are well-trained, special attention will have to
be given to their training to ensure they have the information and skills they
need. Each year SMS would spend about one-third of their time working with
researchers at Field Trial Stations and gathering information from research
facilities and other sources for use in their VLEW training sessions. This
would involve visits of SMS to Field Trial Stations, AAU, fields of progressive
farmers and other sources. In addition each SMS should receive one or two week's
intensive training each year in subjects related to their speciality. These
aspects of SMS work and training would be coordinated at District and Head-
quarters level. Training would be arranged either in Assam or in other parts
of India. Special short courses for other senior staff (for example in admin-
istration and management, extension methods, etc.) would be provided as necessary.
I1   Field trials stations would also be used for directly training farmers.
Under the Dept. of Agriculture's existing program, groups of up to 25
young farmers receive 2-week residential training courses at these
stations (currently called Farmer Training Centers); however, additional
training facilities provided by the project would only be used for
farmer training when not required to train extension staff.



ANNEX 2
Page 12
35.       Chart 4 outlines the various forms and levels of inservice train-
ing. ADA (Extension) will be responsible for organizing pre-season workshops
for District level staff and pre-season training sessions for AEO and sub-
divisional staff. ADA (Extension), together with his group of senior subject
matter specialists, will also be responsible for organizing special short
courses for SMS and other senior staff. The Agricultural Information Officer,
under the direction of the DAO and in consultation with the district and sub-
divisional SMS will be responsible for arranging training at the subdivisional
levels. In-service training costs are included in total project costs (Annex 7,
Table 4).
Other Training
36.       Orientation Training.  As the extension system is introduced in
new subdivisions it is important that existing staff in the area understand
thoroughly the new approach to extension and its initial message. GOA's
experience in Nowgong subdivision has demonstrated that such training is
essential for the initial success of the extension system. Consequently a
one week course will be given to all staff prior to the introduction of the
extension system in their subdivision. Trainers would be headquarters
staff and field staff from areas in which the system is already operational.
37.       Pre-service Training.  About 780 new VLEW posts will be created and
filled under the project. These new staff would be recruited according to
current GOA procedures and trained at two existing Gram Sevak (Village Level
Worker) Training Centers. Following a specially designed curriculum which
stresses the practical aspects of agriculture and concentrates on the most
important crops, recruits would receive an initial six-month training course
followed by field work as VLEW. Over the next two years they would attend two
additional three-month courses. By using existing GSTCs intensively, a rea-
sonable match between the number of VLEW required and available can be main-
tained (see Table 2). Project costs include operating costs of GSTC over the
period of the project plus provision for some expansion of staff at these
institutions to cope with increased enrollment. Visiting lecturers from the
Department of Agriculture would supplement regular teaching staff. The number
of new staff required at higher levels in the extension hierarchy are rela-
tively small and can be met by promoting the best existing staff or by re-
cruiting better graduates of AAU. For example, better AEO or M.Sc. graduates
from MU could become SDAO, ASDAO or SMS. Experienced VLEW or AAU B.Sc. grad-
uates could be recruited to fill AEO slots thus vacated. Such newly recruited
senior staff would be given a short specialized supplemental training program
both in their field of specialization and in the new approach to extension
(Annex 7, Table 4).
38.       Refresher Training.  A number of existing VLEW have had inadequate
training. While fortnightly training sessions will help overcome these defi-
ciencies, a longer refresher training course may be necessary. In the initial
years of the project, however, in-service and pre-service training programs
described above must necessarily take precedence. Once these programs are
well established, refresher training courses can begin and those VLEW most



ANNEX 2
Page 13
in need can be identified. The program would last about 6 weeks and would
be conducted at either AAU Department of Extension Education or at existing
Farmer Training Centers. The costs would be included in the project (Annex 7,
Table 4).
39.       Scholarships:  A smalL number of scholarships to AAU would be
provided each year for extension staff who show exceptional talent and for
whom such higher training would be necessary for further promotion (Annex 7,
Table 4).
Administrative Expenses
40.       The Department of Agr-iculture's staff would increase substantially
under the project as VLW with the Community Development Department are
transferred to them and new VLEW posts are created. To cope with the in-
creased administrative burden, administrative and accounting staff of the
Department of Agriculture would be strengthened (Annex 7, Table 5).
Equipment, and Materials Supporting Extension Operations
41.       Equipment would be provided for production of publications, printing
of diaries, and preparation of audio-visual aids. The project would also pro-
vide for duplicating machines and photographic services, needed for effective
extension work with farmers; materials and equipment required by VLEW and AEO
for demonstration aids, field trials, and ongoing training and extension work;
incremental operating costs; and funds for preparation of materials and aids
for training sessions (Annex 7, Table 6).
Transportation and Mobility
42.       A key feature of the new extension methodology and its organization
is a requirement that the entire staff spend most of their time in the field.
Each week, VLEW would spend four days on pre-planned visits and a fifth day on
field trials, demonstrations and special requests. AEO would spend at least
four days a week with VLEW. SMS would be expected to devote two days to field
visits and two days to field trials each week. DAO, SDAO and ASDAO would
spend at least three days a week in the field. Transportation is thus crucial
to the success of the program, and is presently a serious constraint. The
project would provide for: 2 cars, 100 four-wheel drive vehicles, 9 vans,
about 300 motorcycles and 1,830 bicycles. Motorcycles and bicycles would be
sold on credit to AEO and VLEW respectively. Subdivisional level staff could
also be eligible for motorcycle loans.
43.       Terms for staff loans for purchase of motorcycles and bicycles
appear generally adequate but sufficient funds need to be allocated for these
loans which should be made available to all AEO and VLEW, regardless of length
of service or retirement date. GOA are reviewing traveling allowances, required



ANNEX 2
Page 14
so that extension staff are adequately compensated for their travel expenses.
Motorcycle allowances are likely to be raised from Rs 0.18 per km to about
Rs 0,24 and the fixed travel allowance for VLEW from Rs 30/month to about
Rs 40. Project costs include the costs of additional vehicles, their operat-
ing costs and incremental travel allowances (Annex 7, Table 6).
Offices and Staff Housing
44.       Offices would be established in the six new subdivisions which would
be formed under the project. It is important that extension workers live in
or as close as possible to their areas of operation. At present, 42% of VLW
have their own accommodation or live with relatives, 48% live in government-
provided houses and 10% rent quarters. The project would provide for renova-
tion of about 850 existing VLW houses (about half of which are now vacant),
and construction of about 600 new houses. At full development about 60% of
VLEW would thus live in government-provided houses. At the AEO level about
40 new houses would be constructed and about 100 renovated, providing govern-
ment housing for about 50% of staff at this level. Also at subdivisional
level accommodation would be provided for about 50% of staff (Annex 7,
Table 2).

INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agricultural Research
I. Background
1.        Until 1973, agricultural research was mainly the responsibility of
the Department of Agriculture, but fundamental and applied research is now
the responsibility of the Assam Agricultural University (AAU) 1/. AAU is re-
sponsible for state financed research projects and for local implementation of
centrally sponsored projects of the Indian Council for Agricultural Research
(ICAR). Some post-graduate research is also carried out in conjunction with
AAU's teaching program in Departments of Agronomy, Agricultural Chemistry and
Soil Science, Botany and Plant Pathology, Zoology and Entomology, and Genetics
and Plant Breeding. Teaching staff in these departments devote part time
to research.
2.        The First Education Project (Credit 342-IN) is assisting AAU's de-
velopment, concentrating largely on institutional building, staff development
and the necessary physical facilities (mainly teaching). Relatively few of
the fellowhips awarded under this Credit have been to staff whose main duties
are in research (of about 57 fellowships awarded in the Faculty of Agriculture
and the College of Basic Sciences and Humanities, only 7 or 8 were specifically
for training in research). There is now a need to strengthen AAU's research
manpower and, at the same time, promote closer integration of research and
teaching. This would ensure that staff whose main duties are in teaching would
contribute to the overall research effort and vice versa, thus leading inter
alia to the fullest possible use of scarce trained manpower. This can be
largely be achieved by filling the many sanctioned research posts which are
currently vacant (see Table 1) due to shortage of adequately trained staff.
However, some additional research posts will need to be created to cover other
aspects of crop production which presently have no research input.
3.        Principal experimental programs of the Research Wing are:  for rice
(agronomy, varietal improvement, entomology and pathology); wheat (agronomy,
varietal testing and seed storage); maize (agronomy); pulse crops (agronomy
and varietal improvement) and water management. AAU has two instruction-
cum-research stations and several regional research and experimental stations
(Attachment 1).
1/   The Departments experimental activities are now confined to supervising
"mini-kit" evaluation of new varieties by farmers, and field trials on
farmers' fields.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
4.        In addition to AAU's crop oriented experimental programs, the Depart-
ment of Agricultural Engineering is working on the development of improved
animal-drawn implements, and the Department of Agricultural Economics and Farm
Management is carrying out centrally funded cost-of-production studies. The
main AAU campus (Jorhat) is also the location of the centrally financed Agro-
economic Research Center for North East India.
5.        Of the major crops, rice research is located at Titabar (Sibsagar
District) and Karimganj (Cachar District) and jute and oilseeds research at
Shillongani (Nowgong District). The main work on rice at Titabar is in
varietal selection and breeding, maintenance of a large germ plasm collection,
crop protection (especially studies to control rice stem borers and other
pests by chemical and other means), fertilizer needs of different varieties
on the various soil types and the general agronomy of the crop. Similar
work is carried out at Karimganj under high rainfall conditions, on land
subject to deep flooding. Only a small amount of work is in progress at
Shillongani. Research has largely concentrated on producing recommendations
for irrigated rabi crops, using HYV and high level of fertilizer application.
Insufficient attention has been paid to kharif rainfed crops and to providing
recommendations suitable for use by small farmers who have limited access
to credit and inputs. Centrally-sponsored ICAR schemes, whilst responsible
for the major research impact, are more oriented to national requirements
than those of State and regional needs. Even so, some good work is in pro-
gress. Research accomplishments include, for example, the development of a
long strawed variety of transplanted paddy (Monohar Sali) and attempts to
incorporate the high yielding characteristics of dwarf HYV into Monohar Sali,
whilst preserving its disease resistance and other desirable characteristics.
There is a reasonable amount of basic agronomic information available, much
worked out at Titabar and Karimganj. This information covers; seed selection
and treatment, seedbed preparation and management, seeding rate, field prepa-
ration, plant density, rates and timing of fertilizer applications, weeding,
plant protection and treatment at or after harvest for the major crops, in-
cluding paddy (broadcast and transplanted), jute, sugarcane, maize, groundnut,
soyabean and a range of vegetable crops.
6.        The current shortage of suitably trained research staff (a situation
which is slowly improving as more staff returns from training courses) and the
lack of adequate laboratory and housing facilities at research stations is
restricting these research efforts. While there is a reasonable research base
for extension activities planned in the near future, it is essential to develop
effective research programs to ensure the continuous flow of new information
and materials to support future extension activities. New information so
generated must be shown to be safe and reliable when applied in the main
agro-ecological zones within Assam and to be financially attractive to the
farmer. The project provides support for such research activities at the field
trials level and at AAU research stations.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
II. Project Research Support
A. Introduction
7.        The research program would comprise two separate but complementary
components. The first would provide for the establishment of nine field trial
stations and a headquarters unit at AAU, Jorhat, to serve the main agroecolo-
gical zones in the plains area of Assam and to provide centers for training
of extension staff. The second, would strengthen two main commodity research
stations of AAU and one sub-station covering (a) rice and (b) jute, oilseeds,
pulses and wheat.
B. Field Trials Stations
Objectives:
8.        The objective of setting up nine Field Trials Stations (FTS) is to
provide the reorganized extension service with technical recommendations
matched to the locality, and resources and skills of farmers. Under the
strategy to be adopted for increasing production, farmers would receive ex-
tension advice based initially on low-cost methods of yield improvement using
better cultural practices and later, as skills develop, on more costly tech-
niques such as use of purchased HYV seeds, fertilizers and pesticides. Ini-
tial recommendations would be relatively easy to formulate from existing
knowledge; the first task of the FTS would be to quantify the effectiveness
and reliability of these recommendations. Subsequently, the more difficult
task of refining the next set of recommendations would be undertaken. Work
on FTS would rely heavily on the flow of information from AAU and its commo-
dity oriented research stations to be strengthened under the project.
Sites
9.        In Assam the varying combinations of soils, climate, and suscepti-
bility to seasonal flooding create a number of broad agro-ecological zones
with different seasonal cropping patterns, intensities and practices for
which different extension approaches are necessary. The main zones are:
Main Agro-Ecological Zones
Administrative
Zone                                    Site of FTS      Sub-Division
Upper Brahmaputra Valley Region                   Tinsukia          Tinsukia
Central Brahmaputra Valley Region                 Shillongani       Nowgong
Lower Brahmaputra Valley Region                   Balagaon           Kokrajhar
North Bank Plains Region                           Sukiliboria       N. Lakhimpur
Barak Valley Region (Cachar District)             Mahacal            Karimganj
Flood-Prone Areas                                 Patbaushi         Barpeta



ANNEX 3
Page 4
One FTS would be developed in each of the above zones and locations are shown
on the map. Once the initial six FTS are operational, a further three FTS
would be established to provide one FTS in each agricultural district. Sites
for the three remaining stations will be selected during year 1 of the project.
Programs
10.       The program at each FTS would seek to develop packages of improved
cultural practices for major crops appropriate to the agroecological zone
covered. Initially, work would be concentrated on rice, jute and wheat, to
be followed by pulses, oilseeds and sugarcane as stations developed. AAU,
at the commodity-oriented research stations strengthened under the project
would be working on cultivation practices, varietal improvement and selec-
tion, pest and disease control, farm management studies and improvement
of farm implements (Para 21). From this, a few varieties, improved cultural
practices etc., would be selected by AAU for testing at each FTS. Initial
emphasis would be placed on low cost methods of increasing production, in
particular developing cropping patterns making best use of rainfall. The
FTS would thus be the focal point for testing and introduction of new practices.
They would be used extensively for training of extension staff and would form
the two-way link between research and extension.
Project Components
11.       At each FTS the project would provide for:
(i) Farm development. Land would be levelled and bunded.
Fences would be repaired and farm roads constructed and
repaired as necessary. The few existing farm buildings
would be repaired, if practicable, and each farm would be
provided with a godown-cum-garage and an attached covered
area suitable for processing material from experimental
plots and for grain drying. Irrigation, with a distribution
system suitable for use on small plots would be provided for
at least 3 ha, either by installation of a shallow tubewell
or by surface diversion. Since the emphasis would be on
simulating techniques for small farmers, bullocks and hand
tools would be provided for most farm operations. Each FTS
would also be equipped with a power tiller with trailer and
accessories. A list of equipment, buildings and works to be
provided for the typical FTS is given in Annex 8, Table 6.
(ii)  Offices, laboratories, trials equipment.  Office-cum-labora-
tories would be constructed for research staff and their
assistants, and a jeep with trailer would be provided for
off-station work. Equipment would be simple and confined
to that needed for direct support of field trials. Funds
would not be provided to enable more complicated laboratory
investigations to be carried out, for instance, detailed



ANNEX 3
Page 5
soil analysis or isolation of fungal pathogens, as these
would be referred to specialists of the Department of
Agriculture or AAU.
(iii) Staff training facilities. Four FTS are already equipped
with facilities to accommodate groups of 20 for short
residential courses. These hostels and class rooms would be
repaired as necessary and equivalent facilities provided at
remaining stations.
(iv) Staffing. Four research officers would be appointed at
each FTS. The senior, with the status of associate professor,
would be an experienced agronomist and would be in charge of
the station. Others would comprise a soil scientist, an
entomologist, and a plant pathologist and would be supported
by two research assistants and two field assistants. Each
station would have appropriate field and administrative
support staff (see Annex 8, Table 7).
(v) Housing. Standard government quarters would be provided at
each FTS to accommodate the four research officers, two
research assistants and two technical assistants.
12.       The project also provides for the establishment of a special adap-
tive trials cell (para 16) which would be responsible for supervising work on
FTS. It would comprise a Joint Director of Agriculture (Adaptive Trials),
and three specialists, a production economist, an agricultural engineer and a
statisticians 1/. In addition, the project provides appropriate supporting
staff for the cell (Annex 8, Table 7), and vehicles and equipment (Annex 8,
Table 8). Office accommodation and staff quarters for the cell would
be provided by AAU.
13.       All research officers would receive pre-service training.  For the
Joint Director and FTS heads, this would comprise a three-month study tour
and may include one month foreign travel. The more junior officers would
make a two-month study tour within India. In-service training would be
provided for junior research officers and graduate assistants.
1/   The production economist would monitor the financial viability of
actual or proposed extension recommendations. He would form a link
between agro-economic studies at AAU and technical officers at
FTS and would ensure that research was adequately related to
socio-economic constraints faced by small farmers. The agricultural
engineer would work with the Agricultural Engineering Department
at AAU, particularly on improvement of animal-drawn implements
and hand tools for final testing at FTS. The statistician would
analyze trial results and, organize simple socio-economic surveys
dealing with questions which emerge from FTS programs related to
farmers' resources or attitudes.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
Project Costs and Phasing
14.       Phasing of expenditures is shown in Annex 8, Table 1.  In view
of the modest size of the program, it is assumed that construction and equip-
ment purchases for the first six FTS would be completed by Project year 3.
The cell at Jorhat would be fully operational by the end of Project year 1.
15.       Total capital cost would be as follows:-
