Organization and Management
16.       AAU is a young institution with a weak but developing research
program. Because of the urgency of establishing FTS, the importance of keep-
ing research closely linked to needs of small farmers, and the lack of mana-
gerial capability at AAU, the following arrangements have been agreed between
AAU, GOA, ICAR and GOI. Responsibility for administration and execution of
work on FTS would be with the Department of Agriculture, through the Adaptive
Trials Cell. The Cell, headed by a Joint Director of Agriculture (Adaptive
Trials), reporting directly to ADA (Extension), would be located at AAU,
Jorhat. The head of each FTS would report directly to JDA (Adaptive Trials).
AAU would be responsible for technical backup and provision of staff 1/. This
will ensure that AAU staff engaged principally in research spend substantial
periods working in the field. (AAU are currently considering a proposal which
would require such staff to spend at least three years at one or more sub-
stations before they could be considered for promotion).
1/   Since AAU pay scales are somewhat above the equivalent grades in the
Department of Agriculture, it is expected that some staff required
by AAU for the FTS would be recruited from the DOA. The remainder
would be recruited from the annual output of 100 new graduates from
AAU. If sufficient qualified candidates cannot be recruited, the
deficit would be met by deputation of qualified officers from the DOA.
(It is probable that the FTS would be staffed initially by DOA staff
and that these would be replaced by AAU recruited staff as these become
available).



ANNEX 3
Page 7
17.       Formulation and evaluation of FTS programs would involve responsi-
bilities at regional and state levels. At district level, a committee consist-
ing of the senior DAO of the region, senior Subject Matter Specialist, officer-
in-charge of the FTS and a leading local farmer would be established. DAO
would chair the committee and would be able to co-opt additional members.
Its principal function would be to review and formulate extension and field
trials programs and to submit proposals for ratification by the State level
Technical Committee. It would also be responsible for approving the release
into the region of new extension recommendations developed by FTS. The dis-
trict committee would meet at least six times a year.
18.       At state level the formulation of overall priorities, coordination,
and evaluation of FTS results would be the responsibility of a State Level
Technical Committee.  Its composition would be:
Director of Agriculture                               Chairman
Joint Director of Agriculture (Adaptive Trials)
Additional Director of Agriculture (Extension)
Director of Research, AAU
Director of Extension Education, AAU
Professor of Agronomy, AAU
Professor of Soil Science, AAU
Professor of Plant Pathology, AAU
Professor of Entomology, AAU
Professor of Farm Management, AAU
Joint Director of Agriculture (Rice and Wheat)
Joint Director of Agriculture (Jute)
Joint Director of Agriculture (Horticulture)
Joint Director of Agriculture (Pulses and Oilseeds)
Joint Director of Agriculture (Sugarcane)
This committee would meet at least twice a year and be serviced by an execu-
tive sub-committee consisting of the first five officers listed above and the
Director, ICAR Research Complex, Shillong. The sub-committee would meet six
times a year under the chairmanship of the Director of Agriculture and with
the Joint Director of Agriculture (Adaptive Trials) as member-Secretary. The
Joint Director of Agriculture would prepare work programs for the FTS after
discussion with the station chiefs and the Director of Research, AAU, and
would present them for consideration and approval by the executive sub-
committee. Chart 3, Annex 2 gives both the line management and information
flows for the program as a whole including the input from AAU research,
support for which is described in the next section.
C. Research at MAU
General
19.       The project would strengthen two main commodity oriented research
stations and one sub-station located at Titabar and Karimganj (for work on
rice) and at Shillongani (for work on jute, oilseeds, pulses and wheat).



ANNEX 3
Page 8
20.       The objectives of support provided to AAU are:
(i) to build up research capability at AAU in fields
of direct relevance to the extension component of the
project and which would ensure a flow of information,
results and materials into the FTS/Extension system
through the FTS cell at Jorhat;
(ii) to encourage a regular flow of information between AAU
and research units elsewhere (national or international)
to prevent duplication of work;
(iii) to ensure objective programming of AAU's research efforts
to meet the needs of production oriented field research,
requiring full participation of all relevant Departments
of AAU.
Programs
21.       Research programs would have both disciplinary and commodity
components in that each department of AAU would have its own specialized
program, for example, rice breeding, within an overall rice research program.
The latter would be designed to develop rice production systems for specific
agroecological situations and for specific growing periods within the wider
context of different cropping systems. Two main areas of work would be taken
up initially:
(i) the measurement of the effects of improvements in existing
practices without major changes in varieties, sowing dates,
or fertilizer applications, i.e., the benefits to be de-
rived from good husbandry;
(ii) the possibilities of changing cropping patterns (using
paddy and other crops) under alternative conditions:
(a) rainfall only (the most important situation);
(b) with supplementary irrigation;
(c) in flood-prone areas with and without irrigation.
Within this general framework, work would be carried out on:
(a) Cultivation Practices. Techniques which improve crop
productivity would be emphasized for study. These
would include land preparation, time of planting in
relation to rainfall patterns, spacing, alternative
farming systems and cropping patterns, soil amend-
ments (organic and inorganic) and pesticide use.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
Improved water management practices and more effec-
tive utilization of residual moisture would also be
studied.
(b) Varietal Improvement. Plant breeding, screening,
identification and selection of varieties useful
to farmers would be intensified for factors rele-
vant to the different agroecological regions, seasons,
water levels, soil types and resistance to various
pests and diseases.
(c) Pest and Disease Control. Studies would be accel-
erated on pests and diseases which seriously affect
crop production in Assam. Particular emphasis
would be placed on rice pests, especially gall
midge but also stem borers, leaf hoppers, blast and
tungro virus. The epidemiology, prophylactic measures
for control in nursery beds and rice fields, chemical
control where necessary, and screening for resistant
-o or tolerant varieties would be undertaken in joint
programs by the entomologists, plant pathologists
and crop breeders at the stations.
(d) Economic Studies. Production economists at AAU
would evaluate the various cultivation practices,
alternative farming systems and cropping patterns
developed in the studies outlined above. The eco-
nomic impact of results vould be assessed before pass-
ing recommendations to the FTS cell for testing on FTS.
Project Components
22.      Civil Works.  The project would provide 103 new houses and would
repair and improve water and electricity supplies to 9 existing houses. Both
new and rehabilitated houses would be of standard low-cost Assamese type. The
provision of new offices, laboratories, glass and screen houses, verminproof
and airconditioned seed storage facilities would release existing old but
repairable buildings for use as rough stores, cattle barns or vehicle and
implement shelters. Fencing, domestic and irrigation water supplies, elec-
tricity installation, and general improvements to roads, drains and field
layout would also be provided. Details are in Annex 8 Tables 2-4.
23.        Equipment and Vehicles.    As specialists would need to work
outside their respective stations and would also be required to visit FTS (to
take part in experimental work and training courses) the project would provide
5 4-wheel drive vehicles. A minibus would also be provided at Titabar to
provide transport between the Jorhat campus and the main rice research station.
The project also provides equipment for use in the field and in laboratories.
Details are in Annex 8, Tables 2-4.



ANNEX 3
Page 10
24.         Staffing and Fellowships.  At the 3 stations to be developed,
Titabar, Karimganj and Shillongani, several sanctioned posts remain unfilled.
The project would provide 18 postgraduate fellowships to allow staff to be
trained to fill these vacant posts. A further 10 fellowships would help
train staff for new posts created under the project. All these posts would
be at the equivalent level of Assistant Professor (ASP) or above. Short-term
fellowships, for varying periods, would also be provided for study within
India. There are also a substantial number of vacancies at Senior Research
Assistant level but it seems likely that these would be filled by graduates
from AAU when sufficient living accommodation becomes available at research
stations. Salaries of the 46 incremental staff (17 professional and 29
ancillary) would be included. Details are at Annex 8, Table 2-4.
25.        Short-term Consultancies.  About 12 man-months of consultancy
services would be included to provide guidance in organization and management
of research programs and stations (suggested terms of reference are given
in Attachment 2).
26.       Other Operating Costs.  All incremental operating costs would be
included in the project. The estimated post-project recurrent costs would be
about 10-12% of the present total expenditure on all crop production research
(based on 1976 prices).
Project Costs and Phasing
27.       It is difficult to determine precisely the amount now being spent
annually on research on the major crops covered by this project. The mis-
sion estimated these (see Table 1) to be Rs 0.94 M, Rs 0.27 M , Rs 0.27 M and
Rs 0.06 M for rice, jute, oilseeds and pulses respectively. It is assumed
that GOA would maintain at least this level of expenditure plus incremental
costs as shown:

28.       Incremental annual salaries and operating costs would be about
Rs 0.69 million per annum compared with an estimated current total of Rs 1.55
million for 1976-7. This is in addition to the Rs 2.08 million annually for
field trial stations (para 16).
Organization and Management
29.       Agricultural research at AAU is the responsibility of the Director
of Research. He is currently assisted by a Research Advisory Committee drawn
mainly from within the University. This committee will shortly be replaced
by a Statutory Research Council with wider representation; in particular, the
Department of Agriculture of GOA would be strongly represented on the new
Council.



ANNEX 3
Page 12
30.       While the Research Council would decide on general lines of work
and allocation of resources according to priority needs, the Director of
Research will be responsible for formulation of research proposals to be
submitted to the Council. He would be assisted by technical sub-
committees on which all the departments of the University and the Depart-
ment of Agriculture (GOA) would be represented by technical specialists
both from research and extension. Details are being finalized. These com-
mittees would deal not only with research proposals arising from within the
departments of the University but also with proposals coming from the State
Level Technical Committee for Adaptive Trials (this latter committee could be
regarded as a technical subcommittee having special responsibility for the
program of FTS and for liaison with the departments within the University).
A senior and experienced research scientist, probably from ICAR or other
agricultural university, would be employed for about 12 months to assist with
formulation of research proposals and organization and management of research
programs and stations. Suggested terms of reference are given in Attachment 2.
31.       Two senior chief scientists would be appointed; they would be re-
sponsible for overall management (administrative and technical) of the AAU
research stations strengthened under the project. It is important that these
posts be filled by dynamic individuals with a high level of managerial as well
as technical skill. Proposed terms of reference are given in Attachment 3.

INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Suggested Terms of Reference for the Research Consultant
(Organization and Management) at AAU
1.        The consultant will study requirements of the restructuring of
research policy - formulation process at AAU. He will advise the Vice-
Chancellor on procedures by which Heads of Departments at AAU may make maxi-
mum contribution to the overall program; how Departmental efforts may best be
directed towards making effective contributions to production-oriented research
on the University's field stations and, conversely, how existing research staff
may be fully integrated into the appropriate Departments.
2.        He will also work with the Director of Research to evolve work
programs for research stations supported under the project. The objec-
tives will be improving traditional crop practices and defining changes in
traditional practices made possible by recent technological advances, partic-
ularly those concerned with plant breeding.
3.        The consultant will advise on how to obtain and use available expe-
rimental results to avoid duplication. He will advise in defining the attri-
butes of varieties which could suit traditional and experimental situations
encountered in Assam, and on the agro-ecological data needed for the programs
of varietal adaptation, pest and disease avoidance and control. His work would
emphasize the methodology and organization of programs rather than their detail.
4.        To ensure support for the FTS/Extension Program, he will advise on
means of bringing problems defined by the extension service to the notice of
research staff at AAU and how these problems may be divided into components
suitable for experimental investigation.
5.        He would also ensure that the emphasis on production-oriented re-
search not be allowed to distract from essential fundamental research; e.g.,
the dynamics of insect populations under changing cropping patterns and the
response surfaces of crops to plant nutrients when applied in factorial
combinations.
Qualifications
6.        Wide experience in research organization and management is necessary
both in the laboratory and at the field stations level. It would also be a
major advantage if the consultant. was experienced in rice research under vary-
ing soil and climatic conditions.

Suggested Terms of Reference for Posts of Chief Scientist
at Titabar and Shillongani 1/
1.        Under the direction of the Director of Research, the Chief Scien-
tist would be responsible for formulation and execution of the rice research
program. He will be assisted by a full complement of staff at the station at
Titabar and the substation at Karimganj, staff of field trials stations and
officers of the Department of Agriculture. His prime responsibilities will
be to determine precisely what extra data and/or new materials are needed for
the improvement of rice production throughout the State, and to formulate
programs to fill these needs. It is expected that he would work closely with
Departmental Staff of AAU, especially those engaged in climatology and soil
science, in defining precisely the different agro-ecological conditions under
which rice is grown in Assam. He will ensure that research results are made
freely available and tested at the field trials stations.
2.        To ensure relevance of research programs to production situations,
the Chief Scientist will need to analyze and break down complex situations
into specific topics for research and formulate results into acceptable and
economically viable production packages.
3.        In particular, he must consider (i), means for improving yields
of rice grown by traditional methods (especially sowing and planting dates)
and (ii), what changes are possible in traditional farm systems given the
wide range of varieties with defined attributes that are now available on
an international basis.
Qualifications
4.        The minimum requirement for this post is a Doctorate level quali-
fication with wide experience, preferably in the adaption of varieties to
different ecological conditions. Considerable managerial ability will be
needed and should be given considerable weight in candidate selection.
1/   Terms of reference are for chief scientist at the Rice Station.  With
appropriate modifications these would be suitable for the chief
scientist on the Jute, Oilseeds and Pulses Station.

INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Groundwater Development
A. Background
1.        The State of Assam  is best identified as that portion of the
Brahmaputra valley which passes through India. About 850 km in length from
East to West and about 100 km wide from North to South, the valley has been
formed and remains dominated by one of the world's largest rivers. Of approx-
imately 20,000 sq km of land under rice, the most important food crop, about
18,000 sq km are in the flood plain of the Brahmaputra. Most of the remaining
area planted to rice is in Cachar District, adjacent to Bangladesh, in the south
of Assam. Each year, the river, draining most of Tibet and the entire Northern
slope of the Himalayas, carries about 6 billion cubic meters of water to the
Bay of Bengal. Contained in this quantity of water are about 50 million cubic
meters of sediment (enough to cover 1,000 sq km to a depth of 5 cm). Each
year as winter passes the snows from the Himalayas melt and, augmented by
torrential monsoon rainfall, cause the river to swell to ten times its normal
winter flow. This quantity of water, requiring more room to pass, spreads out
across the valley floor. Over millions of years, this process has built up
the valley floor with layers of sand, silt and gravel until there are now
alluvial deposits, over 50,000 sq km, and more than 1,000 m thick. The river
and the rainfall keep these deposits effectively saturated; they contain at
least 5,000 billion cubic meters of water, about thirty times the full capa-
city of the Bratsk Reservoir (USSR), the biggest man-made lake in the world.
About 2 billion cubic meters of this quantity are within 5 m of the surface,
easily reached by shallow wells.
2.        The need for irrigation in this apparently well-watered land stems
from three considerations:
(i) Although average rainfall is high (over 2,000 mm in
most places) it occurs during a 7-month monsoon season,
leaving 5 months which are often too dry to sustain
reliable crop growth without supplemental water;
(ii) Even during the monsoon, but particularly at the beginning
and the end, there are dry spells of sufficient duration
to limit crop growth and yields or to cause planting to be
delayed so that crops are prevented from taking full advantage
of subsequent rain, which constrains cropping intensity; and



ANNEX 4
Page 2
(iii) Annual flooding rules out a monsoon crop on about 250,000 ha
and makes it risky on a further 150,000 ha. The only
reliable way to ensure a food crop each year for farmers in
these areas is to provide irrigation, which can permit them
to plant early enough to avoid the flood risk or to plant
later and mature their crop in the dry season.
3.        Irrigation water requirements for the principal crops are shown in
Table 1.  Because these requirements occur mainly during months of relatively
low temperature and low levels of solar radiation, they are small. However,
once water is needed it must be applied just as rapidly in Assam as elsewhere
in India. What differentiates Assam from other states is that irrigation
is needed less often. This low utilization makes financial justification of
irrigation more difficult, particularly in flood-prone areas unless some value
is also given to the "insurance" which the farmer obtains against the risks of
temporary drought or flood.
4.        The cultivated area of Assam, excluding tea gardens, totals some
2.2 million ha. Of this about 1.8 million overlie the saturated alluvial
deposits of the Brahmaputra. This area supports a population of about 12.5
million people, of whom about 1.15 million live in urban areas, leaving about
11.3 million (some 2 million families) working the land. The average land
holding is about 1.5 ha, but is fragmented so that the average parcel culti-
vated is less than 0.5 ha. In Annex 13, it is shown that a minimum command
area of about 2 ha in flood free areas and 3 ha in flood-prone areas is neces-
sary for a tubewell installation to be a financially viable alternative to
rainfed development. Thus, only about 325,000 (or 15%) of the farmers are
justified in owning a power pumped shallow tubewell of the current design
without forming a group, or selling water to neighbors. These larger farmers
do, however, operate 50% of the land. Other constraints to groundwater de-
velopment in the past have included:
(i) lack of knowledge of techniques, benefits and avail-
ability of tubewells, partly because of poor extension
services;
(ii) difficulties of group Eormation and previous reluctance
of commercial banks to get involved with group loans;
(iii) reluctance to innovate or take risks; and
(iv) lack of priority in programs of the Government
(Irrigation Department) and financial institutions.
5.        The Irrigation Department of Assam has a staff of over 3,000 includ-
ing about 750 graduate engineers, and a similar number of diploma holders.
Organizational and financial perspective is provided by the following table,
which shows annual expenditures of the Department from 1972/73, and budget
projections to 1979/80. Most staff and budget have to date been involved in



ANNEX 4
Page 3
flood protection and surface irrigation works. Capacity to observe, analyze
and monitor groundwater development is severely limited, and additional staff
at the field level would be required to implement any major program of well
construction.

6.        As a result of the factors listed above, groundwater development
to date has been insignificant; there are only about 30 deep wells and about
1,400 shallow wells together irrigating substantially less than 5,000 ha.
Government strategy was previously more concerned with flood protection than
irrigation, and this together with limited opportunities for easy development
of water sources, has also constrained surface irrigation development. In-
cluding surface irrigation, less than 150,000 ha are presently irrigated
throughout the State.
B. Groundwater Hydrology
7.        Assam comprises three major physiographic areas that are also dis-
tinct hydrologic areas. These are: (a) the Brahmaputra Valley, which is
part of the major hydrologic province of the Ganges-Brahmaputra region of
alluvial flood plains; (b) the foothills region of the Himalayan Mountains
lying north of the Brahmaputra Valley; and (c) the North Cachar and Mikir
Hills, and the Barak Valley lying south of the Brahamaputra Valley. The
relatively small Barak Valley watershed is in the extreme southwest of the
State, and its groundwater potential is not evaluated here. The administrative
districts of the State having high potential for groundwater development from
shallow wells are in the active flood plains of the Brahmaputra River and in
the low-lying areas of the valley floor, which are underlain by fine-grained
alluvial deposits (see para 1) to depths of over 1500 m.
8.        The aquifer materials of the upper 100 M (usually below 5 M of sur-
face clay) consist of fine to coarse sand. Their specific yield (the ratio
of extractable water per unit volume of aquifer) is estimated at 15 percent.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
Groundwater storage in a typical saturated sectiSn five meters thick over an
area of one hectare would thus be about 7.5 x 10 cubic meters. In a satu-
rated section of alluvium two meters thick, found throughout the valley at
depths less than five meters, storage of water rer hectare, assuming a lower
specific yield of 10 percent, is about 0.2 x 10  cubic meters.  This is I ore
than half the estimated dry season net crop requirement (about 0.36 x 10 cubic
meters per hectare of irrigated land).
9.        The water table lies at shallow depths on the flood plain even
during the dry premonsoon period, varying from zero depth to two to five meters
below the land surface. Depth of water on higher terraces may be as much as
20 meters but is generally less than 15 meters. Although water table gradients
are generally towards the streams and down-valley to the west, they are vir-
tually flat during the pre-monsoon period. Seasonal fluctuation of the water
table is about two meters in the lowland, increasing to 10 m or more on higher
terraces near the mountains. Recharge of the aquifer occurs during the mon-
soon season from direct infiltration of rainfall, deep percolation of flood
water, and seepage from streams during peak runoff periods. This recharge
is completed early in the monsoon season and large volumes of water are sub-
sequently rejected by the aquifer. Natural groundwater discharge during the
dry season is by evaporation, transpiration and the slow seepage that sus-
tains base flows of the perennial streams.
Groundwater Potential
10.       The potential for groundwater development throughout the Brahmaputra
Valley is high. Rainfall is far in excess of evapotranspiration and potential
recharge during the monsoon season (see Table 2). Furthermore, the aquifer
materials are highly permeable, the water table is near the surface, and draw-
down in response to pumping is minimal. In general, as much as 150 m /hr can
be pumped from deep wells with drawdown uSually less than five meters. Draw-
down in shallow wells yielding about 35 m /hr is typically about one meter.
Throughout the lowland, wells that penetrate 15 to 150 mete5s below land sur-
face can pump water by suction lift at rates of 35 to 350 m /hr. Depths of
individual wells depend upon local variations in the alluvium, and the thick-
ness of surficial clay and silt. Typically this layer is less than five
meters thick, though it increases to 20 meters or more in some areas.
11.       Because the groundwater gradient is virtually flat, water extracted
during the dry season is largely from storage, and without well-spacing guide-
lines, excessive drawdown could occur. A water table decline of more than
one meter would cause a substantial loss of efficiency or even loss of supply
at suction-pumped wells in some areas. On the basis of a specific yield of
10 percent (sediments close to the surface generally have lower storage capa-
cities than deeper aquifers) and zn assumed drawdown of one meter, spread e enly
over an idealized irrigated area (100% efficiency of application at 4,000 m /ha
per pumping season of 120 days) conservative well spacing guidelines are shown
below:



ANNEX 4
Page 5

Deep tubewells with turbine pumps could tolerate considerably more drawdown,
and isolated instances of shallow well clusters would not cause problems of
interference. However, as larger areas become intensively irrigated from the
groundwater reservoir, patterns of well development -- location, spacing and
rates of discharge -- should be carefully monitored for optimum utilization
of resources (para 14). In the meantime, considering that the present area
irrigated by groundwater is less than two percent of the State's total cul-
tivated land, the above conservative criteria may be applied to shallow well
spacing, with development and comprehensive studies proceeding concurrently.
C. The Program
Description
12.       The project would support a groundwater development program designed
to lay the basis for future exploitation of Assam's vast groundwater resources.
It would irrigate 52,500 ha from 15,000 small (3.5 H.P.) pumpsets. The majority
of these (12,500) would be installed on 75 mm dia. wells, averaging 30 m deep
and irrigating about 3.3 ha each. The remaining 2,500 units would be used for
pumping directly from streams and ponds, and would irrigate about 4 ha each.
The groundwater component would be confined to three areas, comprising 20
development blocks, mainly in the Central and Western parts of the Brahmaputra
Valley (Table 3 and Map IBRD 12626). Covering a gross area of about 6,400 sq
km (2,500 sq miles), or 10% of the plains area in the valley, this represents
a sufficient degree of concentration to simplify administration. Because only
52,500 ha would be irrigated, the risk of interference between wells would be
negligible.
13.       Due to limited experience with groundwater development programs in
Assam, the Irrigation Department would be strengthened to act as organizing
and supervising agency for the program. This would entail assisting farmers
with processing of loan applications; supervising supply of pumpsets and down-
well components; establishing statewide unit prices for drilling, installa-
tion, development and testing through annual competitive bidding exercises;



ANNEX 4
Page 6
organizing the drilling program and assisting contractors (who are generally
without vehicles) by transporting drilling equipment from site to site in
tractordrawn trailers; supervising drilling and testing activities (as well
as commissioning); and supervising operator training and initial maintenance
activities by pumpset suppliers. The project would therefore provide:
(i) incremental staff at the sub-divisional level as follows:
(a) 20 Sub-engineers to assist with collection and
processing of loan applications;
(b) 20 Sub-engineers and 50 sectional assistants/
mechanics to supervise tubewell installation and
testing, and ensure provision of maintenance serv-
ices by pumpset suppliers;
(c)  10 water management officers to advise farmers
on land levelling, field channel construction
and other engineering aspects of efficient on-
farm water utilization (including drainage); and
(d) 20 draftsmen to prepare topographic and cadastral
maps in the 20 block project area. As the tube-
well program proceeds they would update maps,
showing new tubewells and areas irrigated.
No new senior staff would be required at Headquarters or
Divisional levels. The Groundwater Monitoring and Tech-
nology Development cells to be created would be set up
using existing staff (paras 16-18).
(ii) vehicles, survey and drafting, and laboratory equipment.
(iii) Irrigation Service Centers in two blocks comprising an
office for block level irrigation overseers and facili-
ties for pumpset repairs and spare parts storage. In
the remaining blocks existing facilities are adequate;
and
(iv) a training program comprising a series of short courses
on irrigation and hydrology for lower level staff of the
Irrigation Department.
14.       Other project components would include:
(i) installation of a valley-wide groundwater observation network
and execution of pumping tests on deep and shallow tubewells
(Attachment 1);



ANNEX 4
Page 7
(ii)  a mapping program designed to provide a clean, up-to-date set
cadastral maps (16" = mile) for each block, and single sheet
4" - 1 mile Irrigation Development (topographic) maps with
1 ft contours of each block (20 draftsmen to be provided
under the project for this purpose) (Attachment 1); and
(iii)  installation, field testing and demonstration of a variety
of alternative and more appropriate approaches to groundwater
development suitable to smaller farms (Attachment 2). This
would include:
-    developing small-diameter well points with either bamboo
or thin-walled corrugated PVC screens;
-    using small (1 HP) electric pumps where power is
available;
-    developing improved manual pumps for very small farms;
-    developing a custom service scheme using mobile pumps
serving several wells.

The Irrigation Department's incremental salary and overhead costs would be

D. Organization and Management
16.       The Custom Service/Groundwater Wing of the Irrigation Department
under an Additional Chief Engineer (ACE) would be responsible for the
irrigation component of the project. The Department's staffing would be
strengthened (para 13 and 14) to enable it to:
(a) regulate the overall program by preparing and main-
taining irrigation maps of blocks indicating areas
believed to be suitable, marking locations of wells
installed and approving locations of new wells;
(b) supervise supply of down well components by equipment
suppliers (G.I. pipe or bamboo and/or PVC screens
and casings);
(c) supervise the supply of pumpsets and maintenance
services to be provided by suppliers;and
(d) plan and supervise installation, including pre-season
establishment of rates by competitive bidding between
local well-drillers, supervision of installation,
recording sub-surface materials encountered, testing
performance and certifying completion of wells.
17.       In addition, special cells would be established to administer the
Groundwater Monitoring and Technology Development Programs. The Monitoring
Cell would be set up under an Executive Engineer responsible to the ACE and
would:
(i) compile and analyze water level data collected by field
monitoring teams and pumping test crews; this would in-
clude collection of data on rainfall, local geology, soils,
topography etc;
(ii) maintain complete records of progress of the tubewell
program including number of wells, density of wells,
and area irrigated;
(iii) plan and coordinate the activities of the monitoring
and testing teams.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
18.       The Technology Development Cell would be smaller and would be
headed by a sub-engineer. It would be responsible for identifying areas
and farmers willing to test alternative technologies, installing and orga-
nizing the technology, and collecting, monitoring and evaluating results.
Both cells would be manned with existing staff. Some junior technical and
support staff will be provided (Annex 9, Table 3).
19.       The Agriculture Department would be responsible for motivating
farmers through the extension service. Coordination between agencies in-
volved, in particular irrigation, agriculture and credit institutions, would
be enhanced as follows:
(i) At the state level by the establishment of a State Level
Coordinating Committee for Groundwater Development under
the chairmanship of the Agricultural Production Commis-
sioner. Other members would comprise: Secretaries:
Irrigation, Agriculture and Cooperation, Project Manager,
the Chief Engineers--Irrigation and Assam State Electricity
Board (ASEB)--, Director of Agriculture, Registrar of
Cooperatives, representatives of ARDC and concerned
banks, and the Additional Chief Engineer in charge of
the program. This Committee would meet at least four
times annually to establish overall policies, set annual
targets and ensure coordination between all involved
agencies. The first meeting of the year, before the
start of the drilling season, would entail a review of
the program submitted by the Additional Chief Engineer.
Following approval it would issue directives to all
agencies regarding the responsibilities to be discharged
by them. There would be two mid-season and a final
end-of-season review of the achievements of each agency.
Policy problems encountered by the credit institutions
would be referred to the existing State Level Coordinating
Committee for Institutional Credit or the State level
Committee on Banking, as appropriate.
(ii) At the district level, through the existing District
Level Coordinating Committee system under the chairman-
ship of the Deputy Commissioner. To review progress, re-
solve conflicts and ensure achievement of installation
targets a sub-committee would be convened including,
inter alia, the Executive Engineer (Irrigation), the
SDO (Irrigation), the DAO, BDO and Assistant Registrars
of Cooperatives for the blocks concerned, managers of
financing banks and representatives of the input supply
agencies. They would meet at least once a month between
October and March.



ANNEX 4
Page 10
(iii)   At the block level, by setting up implementation teams
comprising the Block Development Officer, a sub-engineer
from the Irrigation Department, a junior inspector of
cooperatives and with the part-time participation of the
AEO. Responding to demands generated by the motivational
efforts of the agricultural extension service, implementa-
tion teams would assist with collection and processing of
applications for tubewell installations, prepare supporting
documentation and advise farmers on engineering aspects of
efficient water use (see para 13, (i), (c). Teams would
meet weekly on the same day at a fixed time to process
collected loan applications with authorized representatives
of the financing banks concerned and the Revenue Department.

INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Observation Network, Pumping Tests and Mapping Program
A. Observation Network
1.        To set up a primary observation network for groundwater investiga-
tions to accurately assess groundwater potential, the Brahmaputra Valley
would be covered by a rectangular grid of 220 observation holes 16 km apart,
over an area about 650 km long and 80 km wide.
2.        Observation wells would be drilled (150 mm dia.) to a depth of
50 m, then completed to 20 m with 18 m of plain 40 mm plastic pipe and 2 m
of slotted 40 mm pipe. The hole below, and the annular spacing around the
casing, would be gravel packed.
3.        During drilling, the following basic data and materials would be
collected:
a)   Samples of materials penetrated at 3 m intervals (sample
size about 1 kg);
b)   Detailed description of materials collected:  color,
general composition, grain size (cards with sand sizes
for comparison should be available), sorting, compaction.
c)   Log of drilling time vs. depth of penetration.
d)   Static water level measurements at 3 m intervals (measure-
ments would be made with a steel tape and should be
accurate to 0.3 cm).
e)   Upon completion of the well a hand pump would be installed
and the well would be pumped for 3 minutes, after which a
water sample of one litre would be collected.
4.        After drilling, water level measurements would be made on the first
and third week of each month for two years. Data would also be collected on
nearby wells, if any, including estimated distance to such wells and estimated
discharge.



ANNEX 4
Attachment I
Page 2
5.        The collection of the data and materials described above would
permit:
a)   sieve analysis of each collected sample;
b)   preparation of lithologic sections and fence diagrams
at 16 km intervals, across and along the valley;
c)   tabulation of water level data and preparation of
annual hydrographs; and
d)   correlation of well hydrographs with rainfall records.
B. Pumping Tests
Existing Deep Wells
6.        To determine deep aquifer characteristics, pumping tests would be
carried out on existing wells capable of pumping at least 150 m /hr continuously
for 60 hours. Periodic water-level measurements would be taken at a piezometer
tube located within 2 m of the pumping well, and at 8 other observation piezo-
meters installed for this purpose. The well would be equipped with a 150 m
discharge meter designed for instantaneous and cumulative measurements. Pumped
water would be transmitted by a pipe or lined channel about 250 m beyond the
area of the test to avoid recirculation.
7.        Aquifer characteristics (transmissivity and storage coefficient)
would be determined using standard formulas. Well spacing requirements and
aquifer yield projections would then be calculated.
Shallow Wells
8.        Two types of pumping tests would be conducted on shallow wells to
determine water storage capacity of the shallow aquifers. Special tests are
required because the volume of shallow material dewatered in conventional
pumping tests on a single shallow well is too small to provide an accurate
indication of storage characteristics of material between 3 and 5 m from the
surface.
9.        These special pumping tests would require installation of four
standard shallow wells, on She corners of a 100 m square, whch would be
pumped continuously at 30 m /hr for 60 hours. Periodic water-level measure-
ments would be taken at an array of piezometers both inside and outside the
square, and the four pumps would be equipped with meters to measure instan-
taneous and cumulative discharge. Pipes would be provided to convey pumped
water beyond the area of influence of the test.



ANNEX 4
Attachment 1
Page 3
10.       The quantity of water pumped would then be compared with the volume
of material dewatered to obtain an indication of storage characteristics. The
data obtained would also be entered in existing mathematical models for more
sophisticated analysis, projection of results and computation of hypothetical
stresses on the aquifer.
11.       Following the special tests, conventional pumping tests would be
run on one of the four shallow wells (30 m /hr for 60 hours), while periodic
water-level measurements would be made at selected piezometers. The results
of these tests would then be compared to those of the special tests. If the
two showed close correlation this would indicate that, in future, results of
tests on single wells could be used to predict shallow storage coefficients
with more confidence than at present.
C. Mapping Program
12.       Good maps are essential to groundwater and irrigation development.
In order to monitor effectively tubewell installation, it is essential to
know precisely, the location of each well proposed or installed. Without a
complete system of trigonometric survey beacons and/or a professional loca-
tion survey to locate each individual well, well positions have to be deter-
mined relative to plots of land on which they are installed. The basis
for this would be existing cadastral maps (at 16" = 1 mile) prepared for
revenue collection. For planning, and to record spatial characteristics of
the drawdown patterns which will develop as the wells are put into operation,
smaller scale maps, showing at least one block per sheet, with roads, towns,
villages, power lines and topography, are also required. Again, much of the
necessary survey data is available.
13.       Most of the mapping program proposed under the present project can,
therefore, be obtained from existing maps; only a limited amount of field-
work will be required to produce:
(a) a clean, up-to-date set of cadastral maps (16" = 1 mile)
for each block; and
(b) a single sheet 4" = 1 mile Irrigation Development
(topographic) map, with 1 ft contours, for each block.

INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Field Trials and Demonstrations of Alternative Approaches
A.   Small Electric Pumpsets
1.        Rural electrification is now available in about 8.5% of the valley
area. It is cheaper and more efficient to distribute electricity than water.
Trials would be made in selected areas with electrification to determine the
feasibility of installing bamboo or small diameter plastic wellpoints on
small fragments of land. Because poles on mainlines are spaced at 50 m
intervals, and on service lines at 30 m intervals, the installation of an
electrical network covering any block of agricultural and results in one pole
in nearly every cultivated parcel. If wellpoints were sunk near the electric
poles, it would be possible to mount a meter and outlet socket on each pole
for the farmer to plug in a portable electric pumpset.
2.        The costs to the State Electricity Board of this approach on a
20 ha block would be:

B.   Pendulum Pumps
8.        For marginal farmers, unable or reluctant to take an electrical
connection, or in areas where no electrical network exists, the pendulum
pump provides a possibly viable, but largely untried, alternative. This
is estimated to cost about Rs 500. The only operating cost is the labor re-
qui5ed to work the pump and would be supplied by the farmer. Lifting 3 or
4 m /hr of water 3 or 4 m is, however, hard work no matter how well mechanical
principles are applied, and during a dry spell in April this work may be re-
quired 8 hours a day, 7 days a week. It is not clear at present that farmers
would be prepared to work this hard for the extra produce involved. It is,
therefore, proposed to install 20 pendulum pumps in each of the three project
areas and closely monitor their performance. Since this is an unproven tech-
nology, the first 20 units should be installed free of charge on plots of
farmers indicating willingness to work them for a complete crop season.
9.        The bamboo well point has been designed with a brick pedestal
intended to be equally suitable for pendulum or portable electric pumps.
C.   Custom Service Scheme
10.       The Irrigation Department has for some years had diesel pumpsets
available for custom irrigation service. Why this scheme has not worked
well is unclear, but it is likely that the pumps were too heavy to move around
easily. Also, they were intended primarily for pumping from streams and
ponds, not wells.
11.       Under the project, the Irrigation Department would sink up to 20
conventional (4") shallow wells in a small area, in each of the 3 ground-
water project areas. To have access to the service, farmers would have to
agree to grow an early HYV crop and to pay for pumping on an hourly basis.
12.       Light weight 5 HP Pumpsets would be mounted on trolleys with one
(20" minimum dia.) pneumatic balloon tired wheel in front, two legs and two
handles behind (like a wheelbarrow). They would thus be easily movable,
and one pumpset could serve four wells each covering about 1.75 ha, total
7.0 ha.

ments would be 21,000 m . The pumpset producing 30 m /hr would therefore have
to run 700 hours.

14.       A single farmer with 1.75 ha using this service would thus pay

farmers around each well; with average farm fragment sizes under 0.5 ha it may
be necessary to service 3 or 4 farmers to make a 1.5 ha block.

INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Monitoring and Evaluation
1.        The project seeks to (a) reorganize and strengthen agricultural
extension and supportive research services in the plains districts of Assam,
to generate and to bring proven agricultural practices to a large number
of farmers thereby increasing their productivity and income, and (b) lay
the base for longer term improvements in groundwater development. An inte-
gral part of the project would be the establishment of a monitoring and
evaluation system to: ensure that the extension organization is operating
efficiently, enable project management to undertake corrective action when
necessary, and to assess the impact of the project on the target group. In
order to promote uniformity of monitoring and evaluation of the proposed and
of similar projects in other states, GOI, concerned states and IDA are cur-
rently working out detailed monitoring and evaluation procedures and it is
expected that these would be finalized by July 1977. The following outlines
the broad principles contained in a draft report on monitoring and evaluation
and has been discussed with GOI and GOA; details would be finalized and agreed
by December 1977. A provision of Rs 3.6 million has been included in project
costs to carry out such monitoring and evaluation.
2.        Monitoring would consist of timely gathering and analysis of data
to measure: the build up and performance of project organizations as well as
farmers' reaction to recommended practices. Monitoring or reporting of phy-
sical and financial project inputs (recruitment of staff, provision of trans-
portation, equipment, houses, offices, etc.) would be largely carried out
through the normal reporting system of the concerned organization. For ex-
tension, this would be the responsibility of the Department of Agriculture.
To suit the needs of both the State Government and IDA, a proforma for semi-
annual progress reports would be agreed. It would be necessary to supplement
this inbuilt monitoring (and reporting) system to provide, on a sample basis,
an independent assessment of performance and farmer reaction. This additional
aspect of monitoring should be carried out by an external agency in conjunction
with ongoing evaluation, which would: assess overall impact of extension on
crop yields and farmers' welfare and determine the degree to which the project
is reaching economic, technical and social targets set for agricultural devel-
opment. The purpose of ongoing evaluation is to provide project management
and policy-makers with analytical support that might be necessary to enable
them to assess and, if required, adjust policies, objectives, institutional
arrangements and resources affecting the project during implementation. The
remaining part of this annex refers to evaluation and those aspects of monitor-
ing which would be carried out on a sample basis by an external agency.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
Indicators
3.        The monitoring and evaluation described above would use a limited
number of selected indicators to reflect the essential agronomic, economic,
sociological, and institutional dimensions of the project. Key indicators
would focus on: farm visits, adoption rates and yields. To measure insti-
tutional build-up, indicators would include: staffing of the extension organi-
zation, selection of contact farmers, training and physical equipment. For
monitoring extension performance the suggested indicators are: degree of ex-
posure to extension, visit performance, farmers' evaluation of T&V, adoption
rates of new practices, and quality of training sessions. To measure project
impact, the main indicators would be: yields, changes in croppng patterns,
area under HYVs, spread of new practices, amount of fertilizers purchased,
and credit use and recovery.
Studies
4.        Studies to generate information along the lines of selected indi-
cators would be:
(a) a sample survey of contact farmers, focussing on VLEW field
visits. This would be carried out in preharvest months.
Out of the total of 200,000 contact farmers in the State,
a multi-stage stratified random sample comprising about
2,000 would be selected;
(b) an evaluation survey of a representative cross-section of
the total farming population, including a crop cutting
survey. The evaluation survey should be carried out at
harvest time during the two major seasons (kharif and rabi).
It would attempt to measure the impact of extension recom-
mendations on adoption rates and yields. The survey would
also: provide information on the dissemination of new
practices to followers; and correlate yields and take-up
rates with farm size and socio-economic status of farmers
to assess which rural groups benefit most from the project.
Due to the repetitive character of the evaluation survey,
a time series of yield data will be gradually accumulated.
Surveys in year one of the project would serve to establish
benchline data;
(c) a set of ad hoc studies, for example:
- Study of the selection of contact farmers;
- The use of demonstration plots as extension tools;
- Quality of training sessions;
- Survey of VLEW and AEO; and
- Sociological village case studies on extension impact;



ANNEX 5
Page 3
(d) a special study to evaluate the effects of the project's
groundwater component and to measure on a sample basis
the cost-benefit relation of investment in STW.
5.        An evaluation unit would be set up by strengthening one of the
statistical or evaluation units already in existence (but outside the Depart-
ments of Agriculture and Irrigation). Staff assigned to the unit would be
full time. The unit would consist of two sections: one responsible for
designing and carrying out sample surveys, and the other for ad hoc studies.
At headquarters, the unit would be staffed by two statisticians, an agricul-
tural economist, one sociologist, and junior research officers as required.
Two or three field data collectors would be stationed in each district and
would be administratively responsible to the head of the evaluation unit.
Assistance from outside sources would be necessary, in particular for somelof
the ad hoc studies and funds would be provided for the Secretary Agricultu e
to commission such assistance as and when required. State universities or
other local institutions (possibly the Agro-Economic Research Center for
North East India) would be contracted to conduct such studies.

ANNEX 13
Page 1
INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTIJRAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Expected Yield Increases- and Financial
and Economic Analysis of Investment in STW
A. Expected Yield Increases-
The Extension Message
1.        Extension advice has in the past emphasized use of high doses of
inputs. Most farmers have neither the cash 2/ for these inputs nor the
skills to use them effectively, and input use has been limited (see Annex 1).
Under the project, a different extension approach would be adopted starting
with recommendations that can increase yields with little or no cash input
and progressing, as the farmers' ability and cash surplus rises, to more
costly means of production. Inizial emphasis would thus be given to low-cost
imlrovements in cultural practices by the more effective use of family labor
and existing resources. This would include improved preparation of paddy
seedbeds, improved land preparation, optimization of plant populations and
adoption of line sowing or transplanting, better weed control, and increased
use of farmyard manure and compo,ts. Simultaneously, timing of crop planting
would be stressed, allowing farmers to make better use of rainfall and resi-
dual moisture for rainfed crops, thus increasing cropping intensity of both
irrigated and rainfed crops.
2.        Once cultural standards have improved, extension packages would
be developed emphasizing low use of purchased inputs. The first inputs to
be promoted would be improved or high-yielding varieties and seed treat-
ment, both relatively cheap and zapable of giving marked yield improve-
ments (in view of the relative fartility of soils in much of the plains
area, fertilizer use would generally be kept to a later stage in the ex-
tension sequence, except on paddy seedbeds). Pesticides would be stressed
from the outset on paddy seedbeds and for seed treatment; otherwise it
would be recommended only for emargencies. After better cultural practices,
seed, and limited use of pesticiles, low fertilizer doses (around 25 kg
nutrient per ha) would be recommended and this would build up until routine
1/   For assumed packages of practices.
2/   In Assam, use of institutional credit has been negligible and what
little credit has flowed has generally not been available to small
farmers.



ANNEX 13
Page 2
use of pesticides and higher doses of fertilizers could be generally recom-
mended, after several years and as the final step in the sequence. How-
ever, the above-average farmer who has already mastered the basic techniques
would not be denied more advanced advice at an earlier stage if appropriate.
Expected Yield Increases
3.        In practice, details of extension recommendations would vary be-
tween regions and would be locally adapted by the Field Trials Stations to
be established under the project (Annex 3). It is assumed, however, that
the sequence of recommendations outlined can be consolidated into approx-
imately the following sequence of packages, to be introduced progressively
over a period of several years.
Package la  -  improved cultural practices only;
Package lb  -  la plus improved (or high-yielding) varieties;
Package 2   -  lb plus sub-optimal doses of fertilizer
(about 25 kg total nutrients per hectare)
on local varieties;
Package 3a  -  lb plus 50 kg total nutrients and routine
pesticide use;
Package 3b  -  lb plus 100 kg total nutrients and routine
pesticide use.
Yields of farmers adopting these practices could be expected to increase as
follows (details are given in Table 1).
== about 25% gain from improved cultural practices (package la)
This is below gains recorded in farmer trials (Annex 1)
and are less than those reported by responding farmers in
Nowgong District, where the reorganized extension service
has been operating for two crop seasons;
-- a further 20% gain from the adoption of improved (or high
yielding) varieties (package lb). On supervised farmer
trials with HYV Ahu rice without fertilizer, yields averaged
2.1 tons/ha, almost double the present state average; in weed
control trials on Sali rice, yields were more than double
the state average (Annex 1);
-- a fertilizer response ratio ranging from 5:1 on rainfed
crops to a maximum of 10:1 on some irrigated crops
(packages 2, 3a and 3b);



ANNEX 13
Page 3
-- a 15% gain from rcutine use of pesticides (packages
3a and 3b).
Details of packages and expected yields are in Table 1.
4.        In addition to the scope for increasing yields, possibilities
for increasing cropping intensities are considerable (Annex 1, para 19 ff).
Again, supervised trials have demonstrated the possibility of triple rainfed
cropping by small farmers in the Jorhat area, with double cropping on the
flood-prone areas. Farmers in a number of areas are already achieving a
cropping intensity of about 170%.
5.        The rate at which farmers adopt these practices and increase crop-
ping intensity would determine the rate of increase in production. Only a
minority of farmers could be expected to adopt the more advanced packages 3a
and 3b, even after six years exposure to the extension system. However, if
on average, farmers moved into extension packages l(a) and l(b), yields would
increase by about 35%.
B. Financial Analysis of Investment in STW
6.        Four models have been used to examine the financial and economic
viability of a 3.5-5 hp STW. 1/ Two models refer to a 2 ha holding, in one
case flood-free (II), and in the other flood-prone and hence of lower poten-
tial cropping intensity (III). A third examines the benefits for a larger
flood-prone holding of 4 ha (IV). For all these models it is assumed that
those investing in a STW would be the more progressive farmers showing a high
response to extension (reaching packages 3a or 3b after six years). The mar-
ginal returns to irrigation are calculated in comparison to responsive rain-
fed development, which is also assumed to reach the top packages at full de-
velopment. While it is reasonabLe to suppose that only the more potentially
responsive farmers would buy a tubewell, lest it should be considered that
only the optimum cases have been examined, the fourth model (I) considers a
3 ha flood-free holding where the farmers shows a low response to extension
and reaches only packages la or Lb; in this case the marginal returns are
compared to equivalent "low response" rainfed development. The models are:
Model        Farm Size (ha)          Land type          Management
I                  3                Flood free         Low response
II                 2                Flood free         High response
III                2                Flood prone        High response
IV                 4                Flood prone        High response
1/   For discussion of the need for irrigation in Assam see Annex 4, paras
2 and 3.



ANNEX 13
Page 4
7.        Full details of costs, investments and benefits are presented onlv
for the 3 ha low-response model (I) and are given in Tables 2 (hectare crop
budgets), 3 (financial costs and benefits with irrigation) and 4 (financial
costs and benefits for rainfed development). Models assume irrigation by
purchase of a STW at Rs 7,500 borrowed in year I at an interest rate of 13.5%
(with repayment over 5 years after a 2-year grace period). Other assumptions
are given in Tables 2-4.
8.        The following financial prices (based on 1976 farmgate) and eco-
nomic prices (based on Bank Commodity Forecasts, amended for transport and
handling) have been used:
Financial           Economic
--------- Rs /Ton ---------
Rice                            1,095              2,200 /a
Wheat                           1,050              1,250
Pulses                          1,600              1,800
Oilseeds                        3,000              2,200
Jute                            1,400              2,000
Sugarcane                         100                100
Coarse Grain                      700                750
/a   Annex 14, Table 3.
9.        Details have not been shown for Models II, III and IV though the
major additional assumptions are given in Table 5. Table 5 summarizes the
net increment in annual cash balance for all four irrigated models and their
rainfed alternatives, 1/ followed by the marginal gain generated by the
investment in irrigation.
10.       All irrigated models show a doubling of cash income by full develop-
ment. However, the marginal gains from irrigation on the 2 ha flood-free "low
response" farm (I) and the 2 ha flood-prone "high response" farm (III) are
small at full development and negative for several of the earlier years. The
following rates of return were calculated on the marginal benefit from invest-
ment in irrigation:
1/   It is worth noting that the rainfed models show an increased cash
income throughout the development. Moreover, in most years it would
be possible to cover the increase production cost for one season from
the increased earnings of the previous season, suggesting that seasonal
credit may not be essential for these farmers; hence the strategy
of development to be adopted under the project would be highly
attractive.



ANNEX 13
Page 5
Return with 25% Reduction
Model            Rate of Return            in Benefit Stream
I                    67%                             24%
II                > 100%                          > 100%
III               Negative                        Negative
IV                > 100%                             36%
This shows that on flood free land, investment in irrigation is financially
attractive, even on a 2 ha farm, if the farmer is sufficiently progresssive.
However, on flood prone land farmers with less than about 3 ha would be finan-
cially better off without a STW, unless the STW is subsidized.
C. Economic Analysis of Investment in STW
11.       Assumptions for calculating the economic rate of return to invest-
ment in a STW are mainly in Table 5, but using economic prices. Family labor
is shadow priced at Rs 3 per manday and investment costs charged at the time
they are incurred. Taxes have been excluded. The opportunity cost (benefit
stream under rainfed conditions) has again been deducted from the benefit
stream under irrigation. Rates of return are as follows:
Model                 Rate of Return
II                        55%
III                       41%
IV                        82%
showing that investment in STW is economically sound.

ANNEX 14
Page 1
INDIA
ASSAM AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis of Extension and Research Component
1.        Attributing a precise level of benefits to this type of project
is difficult since the expected benefits of improved agricultural practices,
which also require additional inputs, cannot be attributed solely to exten-
sion and research. Moreover, there is not enough experience in Assam to
estimate acceptance rates with any degree of accuracy. However, there is
no doubt that, the substantial improvement in the efficiency of utilization
of existing resources, which would be brought about by the project, would
generate a high rate of return. To illustrate this, a test has been performed
showing the increases in rice production required to generate a 50% economic
rate of return on the incremental costs involved.
2.        The attached Table 1 shows that to realize a 50% economic rate of
return, annual incremental production value by year 7, at 1976 prices, would
need to reach about Rs 75 M (US$8.3 M). This could be achieved with an annual
increase in rice production of about 43,000 tons statewide, equivalent to an
average yield increase of 20 kg/ha (30 kg/ha paddy), or less than 2% over the
"without project" situation. Experience with reorganized extension services
in other States of India suggests that there would be little risk that yields
would not increase by an average of at least 20 kg/ha, since this is well
below gains recorded in farmer trials where yields double the present State
average has been achieved, and is less than gains reported by responding
farmers in Nowgong District, where the reorganized extension service has been
in operation for two crop seasons. Moreover, as discussed in Annex 13 (para
3), yield increases of up to 25% could be expected largely from improvements
in cultural practices with little additional input other than increased farm
labor. Based on present average yields of 1.0 ton/ha, this would amount to
250 kg/ha. Thus only 8% of farmers would need to increase yields by 25%
(250 kg/ha) to generate a 50% economic rate of return.
Assumptions
3.        The life of the project is assumed to be 20 years and no residual
value is attributed after that period.
4.        In deriving the cost stream, extension, research and training costs
have been aggregated. The groundwater component has not been included as it
will irrigate only about 52,500 ha (less than 3% of the plains area) and,
unlike the extension and research component, cannot be measured against the
total area grown to rice (para 4a) Financial and economic analysis of the
groundwater program is in Annex 13.



ANNEX 14
Page 2
5.        No residual values have been placed on buildings or equipment.
Financial prices have been used for all cost items. The foreign exchange
component is slight, so that shadow pricing of foreign exchange costs would
have a negligible effect on the result and is not attempted.
6.        The benefit stream has been derived on the following assumptions:
a) that benefits required would be measured against the
area grown to rice (about 2.14 M ha);
b) that benefits would accrue as follows:
Year                    Percentage
1
2                          10
3                          30
4                          60
5                          80
6                          90
7 onwards                 100
c) an economic price of rice of Rs 2,200 per ton, based on
Bank Commodity Price Forecasts (Table 2). This has been
adjusted to take into account the incremental cost to
the farmer of producing an additional ton of rice, esti-
mated at Rs 440.
